Manqul vs Majaz and Transformation of Haqiqah and Majaz
Contrasting manqul with majaz and transformation of haqiqah/majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between manqul and majaz in Hanafi usul and explain how frequent usage transforms haqiqah into majaz and vice versa.

Core Idea
In majaz, original meaning matters; in manqul, it does not. Frequency can convert haqiqah to majaz and back.

This passage clarifies two key linguistic concepts in Hanafi usul: the distinction between manqul (transferred) and majaz (metaphorical) terms, and the dynamic nature of haqiqah and majaz based on usage frequency. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly interpreting the Quran and Hadith, as the same word can shift meaning over time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core difference between manqul and majaz
In majaz, the original meaning is considered for correct application; in manqul, it is not. This determines which instances the word can apply to.
Hanafi usul holds that manqul is a separate category, not a subtype of majaz.
2
Examine the role of the necessary concomitant (lazim) in majaz
For a term to be majaz, the new referent must share a necessary concomitant of the original meaning. Example: 'asad' (lion) applies to brave people because bravery is a necessary concomitant of a lion.
The lazim is what allows the relation (qaid) between original and new meaning.
3
Apply the distinction to examples
Contrast 'dabbah' (manqul: only horse) with 'asad' (majaz: any brave person). Also 'salah' originally majaz in prayer, but now haqiqah in Shariah.
Manqul does not consider original meaning; thus 'dabbah' cannot apply to all crawlers.
4
Understand the transformation principle
When a haqiqah becomes rare, it becomes majaz; when a majaz becomes frequent, it becomes haqiqah. This is an established usul principle.
The transformation is due to 'kathrat al-istimal' (frequent usage).
5
Trace the transformation of 'salah'
Originally haqiqah in supplication, it became majaz in prayer, but now due to extreme frequency it is haqiqah in prayer and majaz in supplication.
The supplication meaning is forgotten in Shariah context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یہ منقول کے متعلق بحث تھی ۔ البتہ مجاز میں معنی اول کی رعایت اور اس کے ساتھ علاقہ صحہ اطلاق کی خاطر ہوتا ہے۔ فیطلق الاسد على كل من يوجد فيه الشجاعة مجازا الخ چونکہ مجاز میں معنی اول کا اعتبار اس لئے ہے کہ لفظ کا اطلاق ہر اس پر صحیح ہو جس کے اندر معنی اول کا لازم پایا جائے ۔ مثلاً اسد کا لازم ہے شجاعت جہاں شجاعت پائی جائیگی ان تمام پر لفظ اسد کا اطلاق مجازاً صحیح ہوگا ۔ بخلاف منقول کے کہ اس کے اندر معنی اول کا اعتبار صحتہ اطلاق کے لئے نہیں ۔ لہذا منقول عرفی لفظ دابتہ کا اطلاق ہر اس پر صحیح نہیں ہوگا جس کے اندر رسیب پایا جائے۔ بلکہ صرف فرس پر ہو گا ۔ اور اسی طرح منقول شرعی لفظ صلوۃ کا اطلاق شرعاً ہر دعا پر صحیح نہیں بلکہ شرعاً اس کا اطلاق صرف ارکان مخصوصہ پر ہوگا ۔ و ثبت ايضا اور یہ بھی ثابت ہو گیا کہ جب حقیقتہ کا استعمال قلیل ہو تو وہ مجاز ہو جاتی ہے ۔ اور جب مجاز کی استعمال کثیر ہو تو وہ حقیقتہ بن جاتی ہے ۔ مثال کے طور پر لفظ صلوۃ لفتہ دعا میں حقیقتہ اور ارکان مخصوصہ میں مجاز تھی اب اصطلاح شریعہ میں اس کی استعمال ارکان مخصوصہ میں کثیر ہو چکی ہے حتی کہ اس کا معنی دعا والا بھول گیا ہے ۔ لہذا ارکان مخصوصہ میں حقیقت اور دعائیں مجاز ہوگا۔
This was the discussion regarding manqul. As for majaz, the consideration of the original meaning and the relation (with it) is for the correctness of application. For example, 'asad' is applied metaphorically to everyone in whom bravery exists, because in majaz the original meaning is considered so that the word can be correctly applied to everything in which a necessary concomitant of the original meaning is found. For instance, the necessary concomitant of 'asad' is bravery; wherever bravery is found, the word 'asad' can be applied metaphorically. Contrary to manqul, where the original meaning is not considered for correctness of application. Therefore, the customary transferred term 'dabbah' is not correctly applied to everything in which crawling is found, but only to the horse. Similarly, the legal transferred term 'salah' is not correctly applied in Shariah to every supplication, but only to specific pillars. And it is also established that when a haqiqah's usage becomes rare, it becomes majaz; and when a majaz's usage becomes frequent, it becomes haqiqah. For example, the word 'salah' was originally haqiqah in supplication and majaz in specific pillars, but now in Shariah terminology its usage in specific pillars has become so frequent that its supplication meaning has been forgotten. So it becomes haqiqah in specific pillars and majaz in supplications.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage first clarifies the difference between manqul and majaz: in majaz, the original meaning is essential for correct application (e.g., 'asad' applies to any brave person because bravery is a concomitant of the lion), whereas in manqul it is not (e.g., 'dabbah' originally meant any crawling creature but transferred to horse; one cannot apply it to all crawling things). Then it introduces the principle that haqiqah and majaz can transform due to frequency of usage: rarely used haqiqah becomes majaz, and frequently used majaz becomes haqiqah. The example of 'salah' illustrates this: originally linguistic haqiqah for supplication, it became majaz for the prayer ritual, but due to overwhelming usage, it is now haqiqah in the Shariah context and majaz in supplication. This is a core Hanafi usul principle about linguistic change.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original coinage, based on a relation, where the original meaning is considered for the correctness of the application.
منقول (manqul): A word transferred from its original meaning to a new meaning by convention (custom or Shariah), where the original meaning is not considered for correct application.
حقیقت (haqiqah): A word used in its original meaning without any metaphorical transfer.
لازم (lazim): A necessary concomitant or inseparable attribute of the original meaning, which allows the metaphorical application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of the word 'salah' in Shariah terminology according to the passage?
A) It is haqiqah in supplication
B) It is majaz in specific pillars
C) It is haqiqah in specific pillars due to frequent usage
D) It is both haqiqah and majaz equally

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, manqul and majaz differ in the role of the original meaning, and frequent usage can transform haqiqah into majaz and vice versa.

Sarih and Kinayah: Classifying Haqiqah and Majaz
Classifying haqiqah and majaz into sarih and kinayah, and defining kinayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given expression as sarih or kinayah within haqiqah and majaz, and define kinayah according to Hanafi usul, distinguishing it from majaz.

Core Idea
Kinayah uses literal meaning to imply a necessary concomitant; majaz abandons literal meaning.

In Hanafi usul, both haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical) can be either sarih (clear) or kinayah (allusive). This lesson unpacks these divisions, focusing on the precise definition of kinayah: a word used in its literal sense but aiming at a secondary, concomitant meaning. Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting legal texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
Recognize that both haqiqah and majaz are each divided into sarih and kinayah. Sarih is when the intended meaning is immediately clear; kinayah is when it is concealed.
Sarih in haqiqah is a live literal meaning; sarih in majaz is a well-established metaphor.
2
Define kinayah in Hanafi usul
Kinayah is using a word in its literal meaning while intending a secondary meaning that is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the literal. The literal meaning is not abandoned.
Example: 'long of the sword belt' literally means a long belt, but the intended meaning is tall stature (lazim of long belt).
3
Distinguish kinayah from majaz
In majaz, the word is used in other than its literal meaning and the literal meaning is negated. In kinayah, the literal meaning remains valid and intended, but the focus is on the concomitant.
This distinction is key: kinayah does not abandon the literal; majaz does.
4
Apply the concept of attribution
Real attribution (nisbah haqiqiyyah) ascribes an action to its true agent. Metaphorical attribution (nisbah majaziyyah) ascribes an action to a non-agent due to a connection, e.g., 'the spring grew the plants'.
This extends the haqiqah/majaz division to sentence-level structures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كل واحد من الحقيقة والمجاز ان كان فی نفسه الخ یعنی پھر ہر ایک خواہ حقیقتہ ہو یا مجاز ان کان نی نفسه اگر وہ فی حزب اللہ ایسا ہے کہ اس کی مراد مستور اور مخفی نہیں بلکہ جلی اور واضح ہے تو صریح وہ صریح ہے۔ والا یعنی اگر اس کی مراد ستتر اور پوشیدہ ہے تو نکنایۃ وہ کنایہ ہے فالحقيقة التي لم تهجروه حقیقتہ جو محجور نہیں ہوئی بلکہ مستعمل ہے تو وہ صریح ہے کیونکہ یہ ظاہر المراد ہے. والتی حجرت اور وہ حقیقتہ جو مھجور الاستعمال ہے وغلب معناها المجانز ہی اور اس کا مجازی معنی غالب الاستعمال ہے تو وہ حقیقتہ کنایہ کہلائے گی اور مجاز غالب الاستعمال صریح قرار پائے گا۔ اور مجاز غیر غالب کتابیہ قرار پائے گا ۔ واعلم ان الصريح والكناية یعنی جو صریح اور کنائیہ حقیقتہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں ۔ یہ دونوں حقیقی معنی کے لحاظ سے ہیں. اور وہ صریح و کنایہ جو مجانہ کے دو قسم ہیں۔ یوونتوں باعتبار معنی مجازی کے صریح و کنایہ ہیں تو حقیقتہ بھی دو قسم ہوئی ۔ صریح وکنایہ اور مجاز بھی دو قسم ہوا ۔ ایک صریح اور دوسرا کنا یہ ۔ تقسیم اور تعریف کنایتہ عند علماء الاصول ہے ۔ وعند علماء البیان اور علمام بیان و معانی کے نزدیک کنا یہ وہ لفظ ہے جس کے معنی موضوع لہ سے معنی ثانی مراد ہو۔ جو معنی اول کا ملزوم ہو ۔ یعنی اطلاق لازم اور ارادہ ملزوم کو کنا یہ کہتے ہیں۔ اور یہ کنایہ معنی موضوع لہ کے ارادہ کرنے کے منافی نہیں ہے ۔ بلکہ یہ اپنے معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال کیا جاتا ہے لیکن مقصور اس سے معنی ثانی ہوتا ہے جو ملزوم ہے ۔ کمافی طویل النجاد یہ اپنے معنی موضوت کہ میں مستعمل ہے ۔ لیکن غرض اور مقصود اسی سے طویل القامہ ہے کیونکہ طول قامتہ ملزوم ہے ۔ برائے طول النجار تو یہ اطلاق لازم اور اراده ملزوم ہوا بخلاف المجانی بخلاف مجاز کے کہ وہ بخیر ما وضع لہ میں مستعمل ہوتا ہے ۔ اور یہ ارادہ موضوع لہ کے منافی ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی مجاز میں موضوع لہ کا ارادہ نہیں ہو سکتا ۔ پھر یہ حقیقتہ و مجاز یا تو مفرد میں ہوں گے جن کی تعریفات گزر چکی ہیں ۔ دامانی الجملہ اور یا حقیقتہ و مجاز جملہ میں ہوں گے ۔ فان نسب المتكلم الا اگر متکلم یا فعل کی نسبت ایسے فاعل کی طرف کرتا ہے جو اس کے نزدیک اس فعل کا فاعل ہے ۔ تو یہ نسبتہ حقیقیہ کہلاتی ہے۔ اگر فعل کی نسبت غیر فاعل کی طرف کرتا ہے اس بنا پر کہ اس فعل کو اس منسوب الیہ کے ساتھ ملا لبستہ اور تعلق ہے تو یہ نسبتہ مجاز یہ ہے نحوا نبت الربيع البقل جب اس قسم کی عبارت مسلمان انسان بولتا ہے تو یقیناً انت کی نسبت جو ربیع کی طرف ہے ۔ یہ فاعل انبات کی طرف نہیں ہے کیونکہ انبات کا فاصل تو اللہ تعالیٰ ہے تو ربیع کی طرف جو استاد
Then each one of haqiqah and majaz, if it is in itself such that its intended meaning is not concealed but clear and evident, then it is sarih; if its intended meaning is concealed, then it is kinayah. So the haqiqah that is not abandoned but used is sarih because it is clear in meaning. The haqiqah that is abandoned in use and whose metaphorical meaning is predominant, that haqiqah is called kinayah, and the predominant majaz becomes sarih, while the non-predominant majaz becomes a bookish (?) term. Know that sarih and kinayah are two types of haqiqah with respect to the real meaning, and sarih and kinayah are two types of majaz with respect to the metaphorical meaning. So haqiqah has two types: sarih and kinayah; and majaz also has two types: sarih and kinayah. The division and definition of kinayah according to usul scholars is that kinayah is a word from which a secondary meaning is intended that is the necessary concomitant of the original meaning. That is, applying the concomitant and intending the accompanied is called kinayah. And this kinayah does not negate intending the original meaning; rather it is used in its original meaning but the purpose is the secondary meaning that is the accompanied. For example, 'long of the sword belt' is used in its original meaning, but the intent is 'tall of stature' because tall stature is the accompanied. So this is applying the concomitant and intending the accompanied. Unlike majaz, which is used in other than its original meaning, and it negates intending the original meaning. Then haqiqah and majaz are either in single words, whose definitions have passed, or in sentences. If the speaker attributes an action to an agent that is the actual agent, it is called real attribution. If the action is attributed to a non-agent due to some connection, it is metaphorical attribution, like 'the spring caused the plants to grow.' When a Muslim says such a phrase, surely the attribution of growth to spring is not to the actual agent (which is God), but due to connection.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage divides haqiqah and majaz into two categories: sarih (clear, where the intended meaning is obvious) and kinayah (allusive, where the intended meaning is hidden or secondary). A haqiqah that is still in use is sarih; a haqiqah that has become abandoned (mahjoor) and its metaphorical meaning dominates becomes kinayah in its literal sense. Conversely, a predominant majaz becomes sarih, while a rare majaz remains as a bookish term. Then the usul definition of kinayah is given: a word used in its literal meaning but intended to convey a necessary concomitant (lazim) of that literal meaning, without abandoning the literal meaning. This contrasts with majaz, where the literal meaning is abandoned. Example: 'long of the sword belt' literally means a long belt but stands for tall stature. Finally, the concept of real vs. metaphorical attribution (nisbah) is introduced: attribution to the true agent is real; attribution to a connected cause is metaphorical, e.g., 'the spring grew the plants' attributing growth to spring instead of Allah.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kinayah): A word used in its literal meaning but intended to convey a secondary meaning that is a necessary concomitant of the literal meaning, without negating the literal meaning.
صریح (sarih): Clear, explicit expression where the intended meaning is obvious without needing context.
نسبت حقیقیہ (nisbah haqiqiyyah): Real attribution: ascribing an action to its true agent.
نسبت مجازیہ (nisbah majaziyyah): Metaphorical attribution: ascribing an action to a non-agent due to a connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between kinayah and majaz?
A) Kinayah abandons the literal meaning, majaz does not
B) Majaz abandons the literal meaning, kinayah does not
C) Both abandon the literal meaning
D) Neither abandons the literal meaning

Key Takeaway
Kinayah retains literal meaning while intending a concomitant; majaz displaces the literal meaning entirely.

Haqiqah vs Majaz in Attribution: Speaker's Belief
Rule for classifying attribution as haqiqah or majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the classification of an attribution (isnad) as haqiqah or majaz is determined by the speaker's belief about the agent, not by rational agency or factual truth.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a false statement can be haqiqah if the speaker believes the literal meaning is true.

This passage clarifies a foundational usul rule: how to classify whether an attribution of an action to a cause is literal (haqiqah) or metaphorical (majaz). The Hanafi school uniquely prioritizes the speaker's intention and belief over objective rational agency. Understanding this principle is crucial for interpreting statements in legal texts and everyday language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Recognize that the passage addresses how to classify the attribution (isnad) of an action to a non‑agent. The key question is: what determines if it is haqiqah or majaz?
The classic dispute: rational agency (fāʿil fī al-ʿaql) vs. speaker's belief (fāʿil ʿinda al-mutakallim).
2
State the Alternative View
Some scholars argue that the criterion is rational agency: if the attributed entity is rationally an agent, the attribution is haqiqah; otherwise, it is majaz.
This view relies on objective causation independent of the speaker's knowledge.
3
Present the Hanafi Rule
The author of al-Miftah refutes the rational agency criterion and establishes that the speaker's belief is the decisive factor. The phrase 'according to him' (ʿindahu) means according to the speaker, not according to the intellect.
The rule: isnād is haqiqah if the speaker believes the attributed entity is the agent; otherwise, it is majaz.
4
Apply to the Examples
Apply the rule to the two examples: (a) a monotheist says 'The spring grew the plants' → majaz because he believes Allah is the true agent; (b) a materialist says the same → haqiqah because he believes the spring is the agent, even though it is rationally false.
Also the Zayd example: a lie can be haqiqah if the speaker intends the literal meaning.
5
Derive the Implication
Conclude that truthfulness is irrelevant to the haqiqah/majaz classification. The criterion is entirely the speaker's intended meaning and belief.
This principle is vital for interpreting ambiguous statements, especially in legal and theological contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہے ۔ متکلم مسلم کے نزدیک یہ نسبتہ الی غیر الفاعل ہے۔ البتہ فعل کو اس منسوب الیہ یعنی ربیع کے ساتھ ملابستہ و تعلق ہے کہ موسم ربیع میں نباتات کا آگاؤ اور نشوونما بالکثرة ہوتا ہے ۔ عندہ سے مراد عند المتکلم ہے۔ بعض حضرات کہتے ہیں کہ فعل کی نسبت ایسی شئی کی طرف کی جائے جو فاعل فی العقل ہو۔ یعنی عقلی لحاظ سے اس کا فاعل ہو تو یہ حقیقت ہے اگر غیر فاعل عند العقل کی طرف ہو تو وہ مجاز ہے ۔ لیکن صاحب المفتاح کہتا ہے کہ عند العقل نہیں بلکہ عند المتكلم مراد ہے حتی تو قال الموحد الخ یعنی اگر موحد انسان انبت الربيع البقل کہہ تو یہ مجاز ہوگا۔ اور اسناد مجازی ہوگی کیونکہ موحد کے نزدیک فاصل انبات اللہ تعالیٰ ہے نہ کہ ربیع ۔ اگر یہی عبارت دھری انسان بولے تو اسناد حقیقی ہوگی کیونکہ دھری کے نزدیک انبات کا فاعل ربیع ہے ۔ اللہ تعالیٰ کو وہ متصرف مانتا نہیں ہے ۔ مع ان الربيع ليس فاعلاً فی العقل حالانکہ عقل کے نزدیک انبات کا فاعل ربیع نہیں ہے ۔ وہ دھری تو اس کلام میں جھوٹ بول رہا ہے کما اذا قال رجل جاءني زيد نفسه الجزیہ تو ایسے ہے کہ جیسے کوئی شخص کہہ کہ زید میرے پاس بنفس نفیس خود بخود آیا ہے. اور اس کے ساتھ معنی حقیقی کا ارادہ کرتا ہے اور حالانکہ زید نفس الامر میں اس کے پاس نہیں آیا تو یہ کلام حقیقتہ ہے ۔ باوجود اس کے کہ وہ جھوٹ صریح بول رہا ہے۔ لہذا فاعل عندہ یعنی عند المتکلم سے مراد یہ ہے کہ متکلم جب مخاطب کو یہ سمجھانا چاہیے کہ میرے نزدیک فاصل یہی ہے تو یہ کلام حقیقتہ ہے۔ جو خبر صادق اور کاذب دونوں کو شامل ہے ۔
It is. According to a Muslim theologian, this attribution is to a non-agent. However, the act has connection and relation with the attributed thing, i.e., spring, because in spring season plants grow abundantly. 'Indah means 'according to the speaker'. Some people say that attribution of an act should be to something that is an agent in the intellect; i.e., if rationally it is its agent, then it is literal; if it is to a non-agent in the intellect, then it is metaphorical. But the author of al-Miftah says that it is not 'according to the intellect' but rather 'according to the speaker' is meant. For example, if a monotheist says 'The spring caused the plants to grow', this would be metaphorical, and the attribution would be metaphorical because according to the monotheist the agent of growth is Allah, not the spring. If the very same phrase is uttered by a materialist, the attribution would be literal because according to the materialist the agent of growth is the spring. He does not believe Allah is the disposer. Even though the spring is not an agent in the intellect, the materialist is lying in this statement. Like when a man says 'Zayd came to me himself' and he intends the literal meaning, even though Zayd did not actually come to him, this speech is literal despite being a clear lie. Therefore 'agent according to him' i.e., according to the speaker, means that when the speaker wants to convey to the addressee that this is an agent according to me, then this speech is literal. It includes both truthful and false reports.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule for classifying attribution (isnad) as literal (haqiqah) or metaphorical (majaz) when an action is attributed to a being that is not the real agent. The author refutes the view that the criterion is rational agency (fa'il fi al-'aql) and instead establishes that the criterion is the speaker's belief (fa'il 'inda al-mutakallim). Two examples are given: First, a monotheist saying 'The spring caused the plants to grow' is metaphorical because the speaker believes God is the true agent. Second, a materialist saying the same is literal because the speaker believes the spring is the agent, even though it is rationally false and he is lying. This shows that truth-value does not affect the classification of haqiqah; what matters is the speaker's intended meaning. The passage thus clarifies that haqiqah is defined by the speaker's intent, not external reality.

Key Terms
اسناد (isnād): Attribution of an action to an agent or cause.
حقیقتہ (ḥaqīqah): Literal usage of a word in its original meaning, including cases where the speaker is lying but intends the literal meaning.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage of a word for a meaning other than its original, based on a relation and a contextual clue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a monotheist says 'The spring caused the plants to grow', is this attribution haqiqah or majaz according to the Hanafi criterion?
A) Haqiqah, because the spring is the apparent cause.
B) Majaz, because the speaker believes Allah is the true agent.
C) Haqiqah, because the statement is true.
D) Majaz, because the spring is not a rational agent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the classification of attribution (isnad) as haqiqah or majaz depends solely on the speaker's belief about the agent, not on rational agency or truthfulness.

Types of Relations (ʿAlāqāt) for Majāz
Classifying types of relations for metaphorical usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the types of relations required for metaphorical usage in Hanafi usul al-fiqh and apply the condition of mental transfer (intiqāl) to identify valid majāz.

Core Idea
Metaphor in usul requires a valid relation—temporal, potential, or mental/customary—allowing mental transfer from literal to figurative.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi taxonomy of relations for valid metaphorical usage. Based on Al-Talweeh's commentary, we explore categories including past existence, future existence, potentiality, and mental/customary necessity, emphasizing that even without external connection, mental necessity suffices.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish meaning, concept, and named
Understand the terms ma'na (meaning), mafhum (concept), and musamma (named) to grasp the scope of metaphorical application.
Musamma includes individuals, while ma'na is specific to the concept.
2
Identify existence of literal meaning
Classify the case based on whether the literal meaning exists actually, in the past, in the future, or potentially in the musamma.
If actually present, usage is literal; otherwise, it is majāz.
3
Apply the four temporal/potential categories
Use the relations: mā kān (past), mā ya'ul (future), bi al-quwwa (potential), and note that if none apply, move to necessity.
Quranic example: 'Give the orphans' uses mā kān.
4
Recognize necessity (luzūm) when literal meaning absent
When no temporal/potential relation exists, a relation of necessity is required, either mental (dhihnī) or customary (ʿurfī).
Mental necessity, e.g., opposition (muqābala), allows transfer even without external connection.
5
Apply the condition of mental transfer
Ensure that the mind can move from literal to figurative meaning in a general sense (intiqāl fī al-jumlah); absolute inseparability is not required.
Example: 'seeing' for a blind person via opposition.

Source Text

وضع لہ میں استعمال ہوتا ہے ۔ فان كان زمان الحصول یعنی اگر معنی حقیقی کے حصول کا زمان بعینہ وہی زمان ہو جس زمان میں حصول معنی کے لئے لفظ کی وضع کی گئی ہے تو كان اللفظ مستعمل فيما وضع له والمقدر خلافہ تو گویا یہ قید مفروع عنھا ہے ۔ اس لئے متن میں زمان حصول کو مقید بہذا التقید اسی المغاير للزمان والی قید کے ساتھ مقید نہیں کیا ۔ او بالقوۃ اگر وہ معنی حقیقی مسمی کو حاصل بالقوہ ہے۔ جیسے وہ خمر جس کو زمین پر گرا دیا اور ضائع کر دیا گیا ہے۔ اسے مسکر کہنا یہ مجاز بالقوۃ ہے یعنی اس میں صلاحیت اسکار موجود تھی۔ اسے علاقہ استعداد کیا جاتا ہے ۔ وان لم يحصل له اگر معنی حقیقی اس مسمی کو اصلا حاصل نہیں ہے نہ بالفعل اور نہ بالقوة فلا بدات تريد معنى لان ما کیونکہ جب معنی حقیقی مسمی کو حاصل نہیں نہ بالفعل نہ بالقوۃ ۔ اب اگر ان کے درمیان کوئی لزوم و اتصال نہ ہو تو پھر علاقہ نہ ہوا ۔ حالانکہ مجاز بلا علاقہ نہیں ہو سکتا۔ اس لئے ضروری ہے کہ آپ ایسے معنی کا ارادہ کریں گے جو وضعی معنی کا لازم ہو۔ پھر اس لازم کی تفصیل کرتے ہوئے کہا ذھنا یعنی ان کے درمیان لزوم ذھنا ہوگا کہ وعی معنی سے مجازی معنی کی طرف ذہن منتقل ہو سکے گا اور انتقال سے مراد انتقال فی الجملہ ہے اور امتناع الانفکاک فی التصوير مراد نہیں ولا يشترط ان يلزم من تصویره ای تصور المعنى الوضعى تصورة تصور المعنى المجازی یعنی یہ شرط نہیں ہے کہ معنی وضعی سے ہر وقت معنی مجازی کا تصور لازمی طور پر حاصل ہو جائے کا لبصير على الاعمى جیسے بعلاقہ تقابل لفظ بصیر کا اطلاق اعمی پر کریں تو بصیر کے معنی وضعی سے بنا بر تقابل ذھن منتقل الی الاعمی فی الجملہ ہوتا ہے۔ تو اسے علاقہ مقابلہ کہا جاتا ہے وکالغائط على الحدث جیسے غائط کا اطلاق علی الحدث یہ بھی لزوم فی الجملہ کی وجہ سے ہے ۔ و هواى اللازم الذهنی به لازم ذہنی کی تفصیل ہے ۔ اما ذهنی محض یعنی یا تو خالص لزوم ذہنی ہوگا اور ان کے در میان لزوم في الخارج نہیں ہوگا کتسمیتہ الشی باسم مقابلہ جیسے بغیر کا اطلاق علی الاعمی کریں ۔ یہ تو وہی علاقہ مقابلہ ہے ۔ او منضم الى العرفی یعنی ان کے مابین لزوم ذہنی کے علاوہ لزوم خارجی بھی ہے ۔ لیکن باعتبار لوگوں کے عادات کے ہے تو اس کو لزوم خارجی عرفی کہا جاتا ہے۔ کا لفائط فانه لما وقع یعنی چونکہ باعتبار عرف و عادات کے یہی ہے کہ
(Section) This section is on the types of relations of metaphor. Since it was necessary for metaphor that it have some relation with the literal meaning, in this section the types of these relations are presented. These relations were unrecorded in the books of the predecessors, but I have presented them in an exhaustive and rational division. When you apply a word to a musamma, this application includes applying the word to the meaning, and also applying the word to the individuals to which the meaning applies. The signification of the word in the aspect that it is intended and meant by the word is called meaning (ma'na). If the aspect considered is what is obtained from the word, it is called concept (mafhum). And if the aspect is that a name was placed for it, it is called named (musamma). However, the word meaning is specific to the concept itself, excluding individuals, while the word musamma includes them. The author should have said: if the word is used with intent of its original meaning, it is literal. But he did not mention this kind of literalness because the main purpose is to mention the types of metaphors. Thus he said: when you apply a word to the musamma, intending other than its original meaning, then if the literal meaning actually exists in the musamma? In some times if this literal meaning existed in it in the past, like 'And give the orphans their wealth' – these persons were orphans in the past, but when wealth is being handed over to them they have become adults and left the category, so it is metaphor in consideration of what was. This is called the relation of 'was'. Or in consideration of what will be: if the literal meaning exists in terms of future. That is, when the ruling is being attached, the literal meaning will obtain later in time, like 'Whoever kills a slain, his spoil is his' – this is metaphor in consideration of what will be. This is called the relation of 'first'. What is meant by 'some times' is the time that is different from the time for which the word was placed. For example, the word 'orphan' was placed for one who currently has orphanhood. In metaphor, this time is not considered because then the word would be used in its original meaning and would be literal, whereas we are presenting relations of metaphor where the word is used other than its original meaning. So if the time of obtaining is exactly the same time as the time for which the word was placed, then the word is used in its original meaning, and the assumed [other meaning] contradicts that. So this restriction is implicitly known. Therefore in the text the time of obtaining is not restricted with this restriction of being different from the time? Or by potentiality: if the literal meaning exists in the musamma potentially, like wine that has been spilled and wasted on the ground – calling it 'intoxicant' is metaphor by potentiality, meaning it had the capacity to intoxicate. This is called the relation of preparedness. And if the literal meaning does not exist in the musamma at all, neither actually nor potentially, then you must intend a meaning that is necessitated by the original meaning. Because when the literal meaning does not exist in the musamma, neither actually nor potentially, then if there is no necessity and connection between them, there is no relation, and metaphor without relation cannot exist. So it is necessary that you intend a meaning that is a necessary consequence of the posited meaning. Then detailing this necessary consequence, he said 'mentally' – meaning there will be mental necessity between them such that from the literal meaning the mind can transfer to the figurative meaning. And by transfer is meant transfer in a general sense, not that non-separability in mental conception is intended. Nor is it required that from the mental image of the literal meaning, the mental image of the figurative meaning necessarily results. For example, by the relation of opposition, applying the word 'seeing' to a blind person – from the literal meaning of 'seeing', based on opposition, the mind transfers to the blind person in a general sense. This is called the relation of opposition. And like 'gha'it' applied to a hadath (excretion) – this is also due to general necessity. And this is the detail of the mental necessary consequence. Either purely mental: there will be pure mental necessity with no external necessity between them, like naming a thing by the name of its opposite – e.g., applying 'seeing' to a blind person. This is the same relation of opposition. Or it is combined with customary: there is also external necessity between them, but it is based on people's customs. This is called external customary necessity. Like gha'it, because according to customs it is that...
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a systematic classification of the types of relations (علاقات) required for metaphorical usage (majaz) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first explains the distinction between meaning (ma'na), concept (mafhum), and named (musamma) to set the stage. Then he presents three categories based on whether the literal meaning exists in the musamma: (1) actually present – then it is literal usage; (2) existed in the past – metaphor bi i'tibar ma kana (relation of 'was'), e.g., giving wealth to former orphans; (3) will exist in the future – metaphor bi i'tibar ma ya'ul (relation of 'first'), e.g., spoil for one who kills; (4) exists potentially – metaphor bi al-quwwa (relation of preparedness), e.g., spilled wine called intoxicant. If the literal meaning is not present at all, then a necessary relation (luzum) is required. This necessity is either mental (dhihni), like opposition (muqabala), e.g., 'seeing' for the blind; or customary ('urfi), like 'gha'it' for excrement due to custom. The mental necessity can be pure (e.g., naming by opposite) or combined with external customary relation. The passage emphasizes that the relation must allow mental transfer (intiqal fi al-jumlah) but not absolute inseparability.

Key Terms
علاقة (ʿalāqah): A connection or relation between the literal meaning and figurative meaning that justifies metaphorical usage.
مجاز باعتبار ما كان (majāz bi iʿtibār mā kān): Metaphor where the literal meaning existed in the referent in the past but not at the time of utterance.
لزوم ذهنی (luzūm dhiḥnī): Mental necessity: the mind naturally moves from the literal meaning to the figurative meaning, even if no external connection exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of ʿalāqah is used when calling spilled wine 'intoxicant' (muskir)?
A) Majāz bi iʿtibār mā kān (relation of 'was')
B) Majāz bi iʿtibār mā ya'ul (relation of 'first')
C) Majāz bi al-quwwa (relation of potentiality)
D) Majāz bi al-luzūm al-dhihnī (mental necessity)

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul classifies majāz relations into temporal (past/future), potential, and necessary (mental/customary), with the condition of mental transfer in a general sense.

The Five 'Alaqat in Majaz: Customary and Natural Relationships
Defining the types of necessary relationship and classifying the five relations for metaphorical usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the five 'alaqat (juz'iyya, hululiyya, sababiyya, shartiyya) and distinguish between customary and natural luzum in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Metaphor in Hanafi usul is not arbitrary—it requires a specific relationship between literal and figurative meanings.

In Hanafi usul, moving a word from its literal meaning to a figurative one is not a free play of imagination. There must be a recognized relationship ('alaqa) between the two meanings. This lesson unpacks the two types of necessary connection (luzum) and the five specific 'alaqat that make majaz valid, with examples from the Quran and everyday usage.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish types of luzum
Identify the two categories of necessary relationship: customary (urfi) based on social convention, and natural (khalqi) based on inherent connection.
For example, the relationship between 'low place' and 'relieving oneself' is customary, while fire and heat is natural.
2
Identify the five 'alaqat
Work through each 'alaqa: juz'iyya (part-whole), hululiyya (container-contained), sababiyya (cause-effect), and shartiyya (condition-conditioned). Note that sababiyya and shartiyya have two directions.
The author also mentions that sometimes the same phrase can be analyzed under more than one 'alaqa depending on perspective.
3
Study the examples
For each 'alaqa, review the provided example from the text: 'raqaba' for slave (juz'iyya), 'ghait' for relieving (hululiyya), 'ghayth' for plants (sababiyya), 'iman' for salat (shartiyya).
The Quranic verses are cited as evidence for sababiyya and shartiyya.
4
Apply the classification
Given a new metaphorical usage, determine which 'alaqa connects the literal and figurative meanings.
Practice with the exercise tasks below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عموماً قضاء حاجتہ مکان مطمئن یعنی نیچی جگہ اور نشیبی مقام میں کی جاتی ہے تو حصل بينهما ای بین الفائط بمعنی المكان المطمئن وبين قضاء الحاجة ملازمة عربية تو اس تلازم عرفی کی بنا پر ذہن منتقل ہو جاتا ہے. من المحل الی الحال فيكون ذهنيه تو یه لزوم زمینی منظم الی العرقی ہوا۔ او الخارجی یا وہ لزوم ذہنی منظم ہوگا لزوم خارجی کے ساتھ یعنی ان کے مابین لزوم فی التخارج ہوگا۔ لیکن یہ لزوم باعتبار عادات الناس کے نہیں ہے ۔ جبل محسب المخلقه بلکہ باعتبا خلقتہ و پیدائش کے ہوگا تو اس صورت میں لزوم خارجی دو قسم کو ایک عرفی . دوسرا خلقی ۔ اول کا نام عرفی اور دوسرے کے نام خارجی ہے ۔ وحینز یعنی جب معنی وضعی اور معنی مجازی کے مابین لزوم ذہنی منظم الى العربي او الخارجي ہوا تو پھر اما ان يكون احد هما جزا یعنی یا تو معنی حقیقی اور معنی مجازی میں سے ایک جز ہوگا دوسرے کی جیسے اطلاق کل علی الجز کیا جاتا ہے۔ او بالعکس یعنی اطلاق جزیرہ علی الکل کیا جاتا ہے کا لجمع للواحد جیسے جمع کا اطلاق واحد پر کیا جائے ، یہ نظیر ہے اطلاق الكل على الجزكي والرقبة للعبد یعنی لفظ رقبہ کا اطلاق معبد پر کریں یہ نظیر ہے اطلاق اسم الجزء علی الکل کی یہ علاقہ جزئية کہلاتا ہے ۔ او خارجاعنہ اس کا عطف جزاً للآخر یہ ہے ۔ یعنی ال نین میں سے کوئی ایک دوسرے کی جز نہیں ہو گا بلکہ خارج ہو گا پھر اما ان يكون اللازم الخ یا تو لازم صفته ملزوم نہیں ہوگا ۔ وھو جب صفہ نہ ہوا تو پھر یہ لزوم ان معنین کے مابین اما محصول احد هما في الآخر یا تو یوں ہوگا کہ ایک حاصل ہوگا۔ دوسرے میں جیسے اطلاق محل على الحال وبالعكس الى اطلاق الحال علی المحل اس کو علاقہ محلول کہتے ہیں جیسے لفظ غائط اور قضا محاجتہ کے مابین تھا واما بالسببية یا ان کے درمیان لزوم با عتبار سببیہ کے ہوگا کہ کوئی ایک معنی دوسرے کے لئے سبب ہوگا۔ کا طلاق اسم السبب علی المسبب نحو رعينا الغیث ہم نے غیث کو چرایا ۔ یہاں غیث سے مراد نبت و گھاس ہے جو مویشیوں کو چرایا جاتا ہے ۔ حالانکہ غیث کا معنی بارش ہے تو بارش سبب نبات ہے تو یہ اطلاق اسم سبب على المسبب ہوا ۔ وبالعکس یعنی اطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب لقولہ تعالے وينزل لكم من السماء رزتا یہاں لفظ رزق کا اطلاق علی المطر ہو رہا ہے اور رزق مسیب اور مصر کا نزول اس کا سبب ہے کہ تمام کھتیوں کی مدار بارش پر ہے ۔ تو یہ اطلاق اسم مسبب علی السبب ہوا. وهذ ا يحتمل العكس ايضا یعنی یہ آیہ کریمیہ احتمال عکس بھی رکھتی ہے ۔ یعنی یہ اطلاق اسم سبب على المسبب لان الرزق سبب غالي للمطر یعنی رزق فاته اور نتیجہ ہے مطر کا لہذا یہ سبب غائی ہوا تو اس لحاظ سے اطلاق اسم السبب علی المسبب ہوا ۔ اس کو علاقہ شبیہ کہا جاتا ہے ۔ واما بالشرطية یا ان معینین کے مابین لزوم بطور شرطیہ کے ہو گا یعنی کوئی ایک دوسرے کیلئے شرط ہو گا. تقولہ تعالیٰ دما کان اللہ یضیع ایمانکم ای صلوتکم تو یہ نظیر ہے اطلاق الشرط على المشروط کی کیونکہ صلوۃ کی صحت اور قبولیت کے لئے ایمان شرط اہم اور ضروری ہے تو یہاں ایمان کا اطلاق علی الصلوۃ ہے تو یہ اطلاق شرط على المشروط ہوا۔ وكالعلم على المعلوم یہ نظیر ہے اطلاق المشروط على الشرط کی کیونکہ وجود معلوم وجود علم کیلئے شرط ہے تو علم مشروط ہوا بشرط تحق المعلوم تو یہ اطلاق علم على المعلوم اطلاق مشروط على الشرط ہوا۔ اسے علاقہ شرطیہ کہا جاتا ہے۔
Generally, relieving oneself is done in a low, comfortable place, so there is a relationship between 'ghait' (low place) and 'qada al-haja' (relieving oneself) that is customary. Based on this customary relationship, the mind transfers from the place to the action. So this mental relationship is attached to the customary one. Or the external relationship: that mental relationship will be attached to an external relationship, meaning they have an external relationship. But this relationship is not based on people's customs, but rather on creation and nature. So in that case, external relationship is of two types: one customary, the other natural. The first is called 'urfi' and the second 'khalqi'. When between the literal meaning and the figurative meaning there is a mental relationship attached to the customary or external, then either one is a part of the other, like using the whole for the part, or vice versa, like using the part for the whole, or using the plural for the singular. This is similar to using 'whole' for 'part' and 'neck' for 'slave', i.e., using the word 'raqaba' for 'abd' — this is an example of using the name of the part for the whole. This is called 'alaqa juz'iyya'. Or if they are external to each other, then the necessary quality is not the quality of the requisite. When it is not a quality, then the relationship between the two meanings is either that one is contained in the other, like using the place for the occupant and vice versa, which is called 'alaqa hululiyya', as between the word 'ghait' and 'qada al-haja'. Or by causality: there is a relationship of causation such that one meaning is the cause of the other, like using the name of the cause for the effect: 'ra'aina al-ghayth' (we pastured the rain), meaning the plants that are grazed, although 'ghayth' means rain, which is the cause of plants. This is using the name of the cause for the effect. And vice versa: using the name of the effect for the cause, as Allah said: 'And He sends down for you from the sky provision' (Surah Ghafir: 13). Here the word 'rizq' is applied to rain, while 'rizq' is the effect and the sending down of rain is its cause because all crops depend on rain. So this is using the name of the effect for the cause. This verse also admits the opposite, i.e., using the name of the cause for the effect, because 'rizq' is the ultimate cause of rain, so from this perspective it is using the name of the cause for the effect. This is called 'alaqa sababiyya'. And by conditionality: there is a relationship of conditionality between two meanings, i.e., one is a condition for the other, as Allah said: 'And Allah would never waste your faith' (Surah Al-Baqarah: 143), i.e., your prayer. This is an example of using the condition for the conditioned, because faith is an important and necessary condition for the validity and acceptance of prayer. So here 'iman' is applied to 'salat', so it is using the condition for the conditioned. And like knowledge for the known: this is an example of using the conditioned for the condition, because the existence of the known is a condition for the existence of knowledge, so knowledge is conditioned upon the realization of the known. So this is using 'ilm' for 'ma'lum', i.e., using the conditioned for the condition. This is called 'alaqa shartiyya'.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul text explains the classification of 'alaqat (relations) that justify metaphorical usage (majaz). The author begins by distinguishing two types of necessary relationship (luzum) between literal and figurative meanings: customary (urfi) and natural (khalqi). Customary relationship is based on social convention, while natural relationship is based on inherent connection. Then he describes five specific 'alaqat: 1) Juz'iyya: when one meaning is a part of the other, e.g., using 'neck' (raqaba) to mean 'slave' (since the slave is partly identified by his neck). 2) Hulul: when one meaning is contained in the other, e.g., using 'ghait' (low place) for the act of relieving oneself, because the action occurs in that place. 3) Sababiyya: causal relationship, e.g., using 'rain' (ghayth) for the plants it causes, or 'provision' (rizq) for rain. The author notes that the same Quranic verse can be interpreted both ways depending on perspective. 4) Shartiyya: conditional relationship, e.g., using 'faith' (iman) for 'prayer' (salat) because faith is a condition for prayer. He also gives the reverse example of 'knowledge' for 'known'. These classifications are foundational in Hanafi balaghah and usul for understanding how words can be used metaphorically while maintaining linguistic and legal precision.

Key Terms
لزوم (luzum): The necessary relationship between two meanings that enables metaphorical transfer.
علاقہ جزئیہ (alaqa juz'iyya): The relation where one meaning is a part of the other, allowing usage of the whole for the part or vice versa.
علاقہ سببیہ (alaqa sababiyya): The relation of causation between two meanings, allowing usage of the cause for the effect or vice versa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which 'alaqa is illustrated by using the word 'ghayth' (rain) to mean 'plants'?
A) Juz'iyya
B) Hululiyya
C) Sababiyya
D) Shartiyya

Key Takeaway
Majaz in Hanafi usul is always grounded in a specific, recognized relationship ('alaqa) between literal and figurative meanings; understanding these 'alaqat is essential for correct istinbat.

The 'Alaqa of Wasfiyya (Isti'ara) and Bidirectional Majaz
Defining isti'ara and explaining mutual majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define isti'ara as a qualitative 'alaqa, state its condition of a clear quality, and explain the principle of mutual majaz (majaz al-tarafayn) with examples from cause-effect and whole-part.

Core Idea
Isti'ara requires a clear quality; majaz can be mutual when both sides have a claim to primacy.

This passage from al-Talweeh explains the crucial distinction between a simple qualitative relation (wasfiyya) and the conditions under which metaphorical usage (isti'ara) becomes legitimate. Beyond defining isti'ara, the author presents a subtle analysis of bidirectional majaz (majaz al-tarafayn), showing that in certain relationships like cause-effect and whole-part, both terms can be metaphorically extended to each other because each can be considered primary in a different respect. This principle has significant implications for interpreting legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'alaqa of wasfiyya
Understand that isti'ara is based on a prominent quality (wasf bayyin wadih) shared between literal and figurative meanings. The quality must be clear and evident.
The example given is 'asad' (lion) for a brave man, because courage is a clear quality of lions.
2
List the conditions for valid isti'ara
The quality (wasf) must be 'bayyin wadih' (clear and evident). If it is obscure or not well-known, the metaphor is not considered a valid isti'ara.
This condition ensures that the figurative usage is immediately recognizable.
3
Understand the foundation of majaz in luzum
Majaz is built on the relation of necessity (luzum): a term for the requisite (malzum) is used to mean its necessary property (lazim). The requisite is primary, the property is secondary.
In the lion example, the word 'asad' (requisite) is used to mean courage (necessary property).
4
Analyze the principle of mutual majaz (majaz al-tarafayn)
If each side can be considered primary in a different respect, the metaphorical usage can work both ways. For cause-effect: cause is primary in existence, effect is primary as the intended goal, so both directions are valid. For whole-part: whole is primary in meaning, part is primary in existence, but part-for-whole is only valid when the part entails the whole.
The restriction prevents arbitrary part-for-whole metaphors, ensuring precision in legal interpretation.
5
Apply the restriction on part-for-whole
Using the name of a part to refer to the whole is permissible only when the part necessarily entails the whole (mustalzim), such as 'raqaba' (neck) for the whole slave, or 'ra's' (head) for the whole person.
This is not universally valid; it requires the part to be essential to the whole's existence or identity.

Or it is a quality: the necessary quality for the requisite, and that is isti'ara (metaphor). It is called isti'ara, but it has a condition that this quality be clear and evident, like 'asad' (lion). If you use 'asad' and intend its necessary property, i.e., courage, then this necessary property is a clear quality of the lion. So using 'asad' for Zayd on the basis that he is courageous is called isti'ara. Like 'I saw a lion' — this is called 'alaqa wasfiyya. So according to the author, the relations of majaz are nine: (1) al-kawn, (2) al-isti'dad al-qabila, (3) al-juz'iyya, (4) al-hulul, (5) al-sababiyya, (6) al-shartiyya, (7) al-wasfiyya. When you know that the foundation of majaz is that the name of the requisite is applied to the necessary property, i.e., using the requisite and intending the necessary property. And necessity is primary and the necessary property is secondary. If essentiality and subsidiarity can be established from both sides, then majaz can be used bilaterally. Like cause with effect: cause and effect, and effect is something that is the final cause of the cause. Although the cause holds the rank of primary because the effect needs it, but the effect that is intended by itself becomes like the final cause and is considered primary. The final cause, although in external existence is an effect of the agent and later in existence, but in mental existence, which is conceived in the mind of the agent, it becomes the cause of the agent's agency and precedes in existence. So in such cause and effect, essentiality and subsidiarity can be established from both sides, and majaz can be used bilaterally. Using the cause to intend the effect and vice versa — both are correct. And like the part with the whole: the part is subordinate to the whole. That is, the word that is coined for the whole, the part is subordinate in relation to it. Because the part is understood from this word following the whole. That is, when the word coined for the whole conveys the whole, then the part is also understood subsidiary to that whole. So in this respect, the whole is primary. So it is correct to use this word coined for the whole and intend its part. And the whole is needful of the part in its existence, because the existence of the whole cannot be without the existence of the parts. So then the part becomes primary. So in this respect, the part becomes like the primary. So it is correct to use the word coined for the part and intend the whole — this is bilateral majaz. So using the name of the whole for the part is consistent and universal, but its opposite (using the name of the part for the whole) is not consistent and universal, meaning it is not correct to intend the whole from every part. Rather, it is permissible only in cases where the part entails the whole, like 'neck' and 'head', for example 'neck' and...
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of 'alaqat in majaz, focusing on wasfiyya (qualitative relation) which is isti'ara. The author explains that isti'ara occurs when a word is used for something due to a salient quality, like calling a brave man a 'lion' because courage is a clear quality of lions. He states that according to the author (al-Sadr al-Shari'ah), there are nine 'alaqat but only seven are mentioned here. Then the text shifts to a general principle: majaz is built on the relationship of necessary (luzum), where a term for the requisite is used to mean its necessary property. If the relationship is such that both sides can be considered primary in different respects, then majaz can work bilaterally — i.e., both applying the cause to the effect and vice versa. He gives the example of cause and effect: although the cause is primary in existence, the effect can be primary in being the intended goal, so both directions are valid. Similarly, with whole and part: the whole is primary because parts are understood from it, but parts are also primary because the whole depends on them for existence. However, the author notes a restriction: using the part for the whole is not universally valid; it is only permissible when the part entails the whole, such as 'neck' (raqaba) for the entire slave, or 'head' for the whole body. This nuanced treatment reflects the Hanafi concern for precision in legal interpretation.

Key Terms
استعارہ (isti'ara): A metaphorical use based on a clear quality shared between the literal and figurative meanings.
علاقہ وصفیہ (alaqa wasfiyya): The relation based on a prominent quality, allowing isti'ara.
مجاز طرفین (majaz al-tarafayn): Bidirectional metaphorical usage where both sides can be used to refer to each other due to mutual essentiality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case is it permissible to use the name of the part to refer to the whole according to the passage?
A) Always, because part implies whole
B) Only when the part necessarily entails the whole, like 'neck' for 'slave'
C) Never, because part is subordinate to whole
D) Only in the case of cause and effect

Key Takeaway
Isti'ara requires a clear quality; mutual majaz is valid when both sides can be primary in different respects, but part-for-whole is restricted to parts that entail the whole.

Metaphorical Transfer Between Cause and Effect: Talaq and Itaq
Explaining the majaz between cause and effect with talaq and itaq examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'sabab mahd' to determine when it is permissible to use the name of a cause for its effect in legal language.

Core Idea
When a cause is a mere cause (sabab mahd), language flows only from cause to effect, not reverse.

This lesson explores a key usul al-fiqh principle: the metaphorical relationship between cause (sabab) and effect (musabbab) in shar'i language. Using the classic example of talaq (divorce) and itaq (emancipation), the Hanafi school establishes a clear rule about when one legal term can be used to mean another. Understanding this principle will sharpen your ability to analyze legal language and recognize the limits of metaphor in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish cause types
Differentiate between a cause that is legislatively intended for its effect (sabab shar'i) and a mere cause (sabab mahd) that leads to the effect but is not legislated for it.
Example: Ownership of corpus (milk al-raqaba) is a mere cause for ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'a).
2
Apply the directionality rule
For a mere cause, only using the name of the cause for the effect is valid (itlaq al-sabab 'ala al-musabbab). The reverse is invalid.
Hence, 'itaq' (cause) can be used to mean talaq (effect), but not vice versa.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
Consider that the opposing school may argue that metaphor (isti'ara) allows both directions based on shared attributes.
The Hanafi response: for shar'i metaphor, the shared attribute must be the legislative purpose, not just any common feature.
4
Verify with conditions of metaphor
Check that talaq and itaq, though both are isqatat (removals) with saraya and luzum, differ in their underlying shar'i purposes.
Talaq ends marriage; itaq grants freedom. No overlap in legislative intent.
5
Conclude the Hanafi ruling
Emancipation is not established by the word 'talaq'. Using 'itaq' for divorce is valid (subject to intention).
This is thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference between schools.

دے توکل مطلقہ ہو جائیگی۔ اسی طرح لونڈی کا بعض حصہ ازاد کرے تو کل میں سرایتہ کر جائے گا ۔ اور نصف قصاص معاف کرے تو کل معاف ہو جائے گا اور لزوم سے مراد یہ ہے کہ وہ نسخ کو قبول نہیں کرتے بلکہ یہ لازم ہو جائے ہیں جس سے انحراف و رجوع نہیں ہو سکتا ۔

انما لا يثبت لفظ یہ جواب ہے اس اشکال کا کہ یہ بطریق استعارہ بھی ثابت نہیں ہو سکتا. لا فعالا تصور بکل وصف کہ استعارہ ہر وصف کے اشتراک کی بنیاد پر ثابت نہیں ہو سکتا۔ جیسے استعارہ سمامہ کا برائے ارض صیحیح نہیں ہے۔ حالانکہ دونوں وصف وجود میں شریک ہیں ۔ بلکہ استفادہ کے لئے شرط ہے کہ اشتراک وصف مشہور ہیں ہو اور اس وصف کو مستعار منہ یعنی مشبہ بہ کے ساتھ زیادہ اختصاص ہو اور شرعیات میں ضرورت ہے کہ اشتراک معنی مشروعیہ میں ہو کہ کیف شریع یعنی شریعت نے اس تعریف کو جس غرض و مقصد کے لئے مشروع کیا ہے اس غرض میں اشتراک ہو تو پھر ایک کا استعارہ دوسرے کے لئے ہو سکے گا۔ ولاد اتصال بينهما فيه اس معنی مشروعيه کہ کیف شرع میں طلاق اور عناق کے اندر اتصال و اشتراک موجود نہیں ہے۔ لان الطلاق الخ کیونکہ طلاق کی مشروعیت قید نکاح کو اٹھانے اور ختم کرنے کے لئے ہے ۔ اور اعتاق کی مشروعیتہ ایک قوۃہ شرعیہ کو ثابت کرنے کی خاطر ہے۔ کیونکہ منقولات شرعیہ میں معانی لغویہ کا اعتبار اور رعایت کی جاتی ہے ۔ اور عشق کا معنی لغوی فوق ہے ۔ جیسے عشق انطاکہ اس وقت بولا جاتا ہے جب وہ قوی ہو کر اپنے آشیانہ سے اڑ جائے اور اسی سے ماخوذ ہے عتاق الطیر جو قوی پرندوں پر بولا جاتا ہے اور عتقت البکر اس وقت کہتے ہیں کہ باکرہ لڑکی بالغ ہو جائے اور قوی ہو جاتے فنقله الشرع تو شریعہ نے اس لفظ عشق کو قوۃ مخصوصہ کے لئے نقل کیا ہے . تو طلاق اور عتاق کو وصف مشروعیت میں اشتراک نہ ہوا لہذا استعارہ صحیح نہیں ہو گا ۔ فان قيل الاعتاق ازالة الملك یعنی اگر یہ سوال کیا جائے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ رح کے نزدیک اعتاق ازالہ ملک کا نام ہے جیسے تجزی اعتاق کے مسئلہ میں یہ معروف ہے اور طلاق ازالہ قید کو کہتے ہیں
This was that applying the name of the effect to the cause occurs when the cause is a cause legislatively intended for the ruling. But when it is a cause, i.e., when it is a mere cause and not legislatively intended for the ruling, then it does not reverse, meaning that at that time applying the name of the effect to the cause is not correct. However, applying the cause to the effect will be correct, as we said: when originality and derivative nature can be from both sides, then metaphor runs from both sides. From this, it is understood that if originality and derivative nature are not from both sides, then metaphor cannot run from both sides, rather it will only run from one side. And by 'mere cause' is meant that it leads to the effect, but its legislation is not intentionally for that effect. Like ownership of the corpus (milk al-raqaba): its legislation is not for the sake of obtaining ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'a). Because ownership of the corpus is legislated even where ownership of usufruct is prohibited, as in the case of a covenant ownership of corpus occurs but ownership of usufruct is not obtained. Similarly, a foster sister can be owned in terms of corpus, but ownership of usufruct cannot be obtained. It should be clear that a foster sister can be owned. This is not included in 'whoever owns a womb relative, it is a sin' because 'womb relative' refers to blood relationship, not foster relationship. So when ownership of corpus is a mere cause for ownership of usufruct, then the metaphor of ownership of corpus will be for ownership of usufruct. Like the words 'sale' and 'dower' can be used for marriage but not the reverse. Because in a mere cause, metaphor does not run bilaterally but unilaterally. Applying the cause to the effect, not the reverse. So divorce occurs with the word 'emancipation': divorce will occur with the word 'itaq'. That is, if one says to his wife 'you are emancipated', then divorce can occur with that, based on the principle that we are dealing with: that applying the cause to the effect is correct. Because 'itaq' is placed for removal of ownership of corpus, and 'talaq' is placed for removal of ownership of usufruct. And that removal (removal of ownership of corpus) is a cause for this (removal of ownership of usufruct), i.e., removal of ownership of corpus leads to removal of ownership of usufruct, but this (removal of usufruct) is not the primary intent. Since here removal of ownership of corpus is a mere cause, and removal of ownership of corpus is not legislated for the sake of removal of ownership of usufruct. Therefore, here applying the name of the cause to the effect is correct, i.e., applying 'itaq' intending 'talaq', and the reverse is not correct, i.e., applying the name of the effect to the cause: for this reason, the word 'talaq' cannot be intended to mean 'itaq'. For example, saying to a slave-girl 'you are divorced' intending 'you are free' cannot be done. So emancipation is not established by the word 'talaq', contrary to al-Shafi'i, because of what we said: the Hanafi proof is that when the effect is not intended from the cause, then applying the name of the effect to the cause is not correct. And emancipation is also not established by way of metaphor (isti'ara). This is the response to the objection that we concede that 'itaq' by the word 'talaq' via applying the name of the effect to the cause cannot be established, but it should be possible via metaphor. And since in metaphor a shared attribute is necessary, like between 'asad' (lion) and 'shuja'' (brave) the attribute of bravery was shared. So explaining that, it was said: since each of them, i.e., emancipation and divorce, is a removal (isqat) built on transmission (saraya) and irrevocability (luzum). Detailing this: know that actions (tasarrufat) are of two types: one is affirmative (ithbatat), i.e., those by which a right is established, like sale, lease, gift: by sale and gift ownership of corpus is established, and by lease ownership of benefits is established. The second type is removals (isqatat), i.e., nothing is established by them, rather a right is removed, like divorce, emancipation, and forgiveness of retaliation (afw an al-qisas). By 'saraya' is meant the establishment of a ruling over the whole by establishment in part, as when half of a woman is divorced, the whole becomes divorced. Similarly, if a part of a slave-girl is freed, it will transmit to the whole. And if half of retaliation is forgiven, the whole is forgiven. By 'luzum' is meant that they do not accept revocation, rather they become binding, from which deviation and return are not possible. 'Indeed, it is not established' is the response to the objection that it cannot be established by metaphor either: because it is not valid to imagine every attribute; metaphor cannot be established on the basis of sharing every attribute. Like the metaphor of 'samama' (a type of poisonous plant) for land is not valid, even though both share the attribute of existence. Rather, for it to be valid, a condition is that the shared attribute be well-known, and that attribute be more specific to the thing being metaphorically compared (musta'ar minhu). And in shar'i matters, it is necessary that the sharing be in a shar'i meaning, i.e., in the purpose for which the Shari'ah legislated that definition: if there is sharing in that purpose, then one can be used metaphorically for the other. There is no connection between them in that shar'i meaning: in the manner of legislation, there is no connection and sharing between divorce and emancipation. Because the legislation of divorce is to remove and terminate the marital tie, while the legislation of emancipation is to establish a shar'i capacity (quwwa shar'iyya). Because in shar'i transmissions, the literal meanings are considered and observed. The literal meaning of 'itaq' is strength (fawq), as in 'itaq al-tayr' for strong birds, and 'ataqat al-bikr' when a virgin becomes mature and strong. So the Shari'ah transferred the word from that to a specific capacity. Thus, there is no sharing in the attribute of legislation between divorce and emancipation, so the metaphor is not valid. If it is said: in the view of Imam Abu Hanifa, emancipation is removal of ownership, as is known in the issue of partial emancipation, and divorce is removal of the marital tie.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of a usul principle: the metaphorical relationship between cause (sabab) and effect (musabbab) in legal language. The author establishes that when a cause is a 'mere cause' (sabab mahd) — meaning it leads to the effect but was not legislated specifically for that effect — then it is permissible to use the name of the cause to refer to the effect (e.g., using 'emancipation' to mean 'divorce'), but the reverse (using the effect's name for the cause) is not permissible. The key example is using the word 'itaq' (emancipation) to effect divorce: if a husband says to his wife 'you are emancipated' intending divorce, it counts as a divorce. This is because the removal of ownership (effect of emancipation) is a cause for the removal of the marital tie (effect of divorce). However, the Shari'ah did not legislate emancipation specifically for the purpose of effecting divorce, so it is a mere cause. Conversely, using the word 'talaq' (divorce) to mean emancipation is invalid because divorce is the effect (removal of marriage tie) and it is not a cause for emancipation. The passage then addresses an objection: could this be valid via metaphor (isti'ara) based on shared attributes? The author refutes this by explaining that for a valid metaphor in shar'i matters, the shared attribute must be well-known and specifically the legislative purpose (ma'na shar'i). Although both talaq and itaq are removals (isqatat) that share characteristics like saraya (transmissibility) and luzum (irrevocability), their ultimate legislative purposes differ. Talaq is legislated to end a marriage, while itaq is legislated to grant freedom and establish a legal capacity. Their literal meanings also differ: itaq comes from strength, talaq from release. Therefore, the metaphor is not valid. The passage concludes by noting the Hanafi position (contrary to Shafi'i) that emancipation cannot be effected by the word 'talaq', based on this linguistic principle.

Key Terms
سبب محض (sabab mahd): A cause that leads to the effect but is not legislatively intended for that effect.
اطلاق السبب على المسبب (itlaq al-sabab 'ala al-musabbab): Using the name of the cause to refer to the effect metaphorically.
سرایة (saraya): Spreading of a ruling from a part to the whole, as in divorce of half the wife effecting full divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle discussed, which of the following is correct regarding using the words 'talaq' and 'itaq'?
A) Using 'itaq' for divorce is valid, and using 'talaq' for emancipation is also valid.
B) Using 'itaq' for divorce is valid, but using 'talaq' for emancipation is not valid.
C) Using 'talaq' for emancipation is valid, but using 'itaq' for divorce is not valid.
D) Neither is valid because they are different categories.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a cause is a mere cause (sabab mahd), only using the name of the cause for the effect is valid, not vice versa, as illustrated by talaq and itaq.

Metaphor in Contracts: One-Way Nature and Valid Examples
Analyzing metaphor examples in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify instances of majāz in contractual examples and apply the rule that metaphor is one-way (unidirectional).

Core Idea
Metaphor in contracts is one-way: you can use 'sale' for lease but not 'lease' for sale.

The Hanafi usul tradition analyzes metaphorical language in contracts. The text presents examples like marriage with 'gift' and lease with 'sale', explaining why these are valid metaphors but why certain phrasings (e.g., adding 'sale' to benefits) are invalid. Crucially, metaphor is unidirectional—a word can be used for something related but not vice versa. We will dissect these examples and understand the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples of majāz mentioned
List the three key examples: marriage using 'gift', divorce using 'love', lease using 'sale'. Note: these are not literal but metaphorical.
2
Analyze why they are majāz, not cause-effect
The text argues that the 'gift' is not a cause for the effects of marriage (e.g., right to divorce). Similarly, 'sale' is not a cause for lease's effects. They are metaphors due to a necessary relationship (e.g., both involve transfer of ownership).
3
Apply the one-way rule
The text states that metaphor is one-way: e.g., 'lion' for a brave man is valid, but 'brave' for a lion is not. Check the examples: can 'marriage' be used for 'gift'? No, only 'gift' for marriage. This confirms the rule.
4
Note the ruling on addition to non-existent objects
Even though the metaphor is valid, adding the verb to benefits (non-existent at contract time) is invalid. The proper construction adds to the substance (e.g., 'I sold you this house for a month').

جب بیج کی نسبت الی العین کرے اور کہہ بعت داری الی شهر بكذا تو اجارہ صحیح ہو گا ۔ تو اضافہ بیع الى المنافع صحیح نہیں جیسے خود اضافت اجارہ الی المنفعۃ صحیح نہیں۔ ثم اعلم ان فی الامثلة یہ ایک تحقیق مقام ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ جو امثلہ مذکور ہوئی ہیں یعنی نکاح بلفظ میں ربیع اور طلاق بلفظ عشق اور اجاره بلفظ بیع ان میں حق بات یہ ہے کہ یہ تمام بطریق استعارہ ہیں۔ اور بطریق اطلاق اسم مسبب على المسبب نہیں ہیں ۔ اختیارات ثابت ہو جاتے ہیں۔ بخلاف اس ملک متعہ کے جو لفظ مہبر یا بیع کے ساتھ کو دی میں آئیگا وہ ان امور کو قبول نہیں کرتا ۔ قبل الطلاق اللفظ على مباین معناہ بلکہ یہاں تو لفظ کا اطلاق ایسے امر پر ہے جو معنی کے لحاظ سے بالکل مباین ہیں للاشتراك البتہ ان میں اشتراک بحسب اللازم ہے اورا سے استعارہ کہتے ہیں انمالا یثبت العکس یعنی استفاده مسبب للسبب صحیح نہیں ہے ۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ استعارہ یکطرفہ ہوتا نظر يعتبر السماع فى انواع العلاقات یعنی صحہ مجاز کے بارہ میں اہل لغت سے صرف انواع علاقات کا سماع کافی ہے ۔ اور مجاز کے جزئیات کا سماع ضروری نہیں ہے ۔ خان ابداع کیونکہ عجیب عجیب استعارات لطیفہ کا پیدا کرنا فنون بلاغتہ میں سے ہے اور یہ اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جب جزئیات مجازہ کا ایجاد اپنی طرف سے کیا جائے ۔ اگر ایک ایک جرمی مسموع من اللغۃ ہو تو پھر استعارات لطیفہ کا ابداع نہیں ہوسکتا۔ وعند البعض لا بد من السماع اور بعض کے نزدیک سماع جزئیات شرط ہے ۔ مثلا کسی نوع علاقہ کی جو جنری اہل لغت نے استعمال کی ہو گی ہم وہی استعمال کر سکتے ہیں۔ اس سے متجاوز نہیں ہو سکتے ، دیکھئے کہ نسل کا اطلاق انسان طویل پر تو ہوتا ہے اور قطع راس کے بعد وقوع موت کامل ۔ یہ انسان طویل اور نخلہ کے مابین مشترک ہے لیکن باقی طویل اشیا ان اوصاف میں نخلہ کے ساتھ اشتراک نہیں رکھتے ۔
But it is not valid with this wording while ijarah is not correct with these words, so responding to this, it was said: This invalidity due to the mentioned wording is not because the metaphor is corrupt. Rather, the word 'sale' as a metaphor for 'lease' is correct, but the addition of 'sale' to benefits is not correct because the benefit is non-existent at the time of sale. It cannot be a locus of addition, so the addition for the contract of these non-existent benefits is not valid as a locus, even so the addition of the lease contract itself to benefits is not correct. For example, if one says 'I leased to you the benefits of this house for a month for such amount', the lease is not valid. Likewise, the metaphor for it, i.e., the word 'sale', its addition to benefits is also not correct. So the lease is only correct when the lease contract is added to the substance, and in the contract addition, this substance takes the place of the benefit. For example, if one says 'I leased you this house for a month for such amount', and similarly when one relates 'sale' to the substance and says 'I sold you this house for a month for such amount', then the lease is correct. So adding 'sale' to benefits is not correct, just as adding 'lease' to benefits is not correct. Then know that in these examples there is an investigation. Summary: The examples mentioned, i.e., marriage with the word 'gift', divorce with the word 'love', and lease with the word 'sale', the truth is that all these are by way of metaphor. They are not by way of applying the name of the cause to the effect, because the gift is not a cause for the ownership of enjoyment that is established by marriage, and similarly 'sale' is not actually a cause, because with the ownership of enjoyment established by marriage, the authorities of divorce, lian, zihar, etc. are established. Unlike the ownership of enjoyment that comes with the word 'gift' or 'sale' in a transaction, which does not accept these matters. Rather, the application of the word is to something that is completely opposite in meaning, but they share in necessary [aspects], and this is called metaphor. The reverse, i.e., using the effect for the cause is not correct. The reason is that metaphor is one-way, it does not run from both sides, just as the metaphor 'lion' for a brave man is correct, but the metaphor 'brave' for a lion is not correct. As for the example of sale and ownership, it is correct because sale is a cause of ownership, and ownership is the effect of sale. And know that the hearing from linguists is considered regarding the types of relationships, i.e., for the validity of metaphor, hearing only the types of relationships is sufficient, and hearing the particulars of metaphor is not necessary. Because producing wonderful subtle metaphors is part of eloquence, and this can happen when the creation of particular metaphors is done by oneself. If every single particular were heard from linguists, then subtle metaphors could not be produced. And according to some, hearing from linguists is necessary, i.e., for a particular type of relationship, we can only use what the linguists have used, we cannot exceed it. For example, 'nakhl' is applied to a tall human, but it is not applied to any other tall thing. If hearing of particulars were not a condition, then due to resemblance in tallness, we would apply 'nakhl' to everything tall, while this is not the case. We respond that applying one thing to another based on resemblance requires that the resemblance be in the most specific attributes. And 'nakhl' and a tall human share in tallness with straightness and the occurrence of complete death after beheading. This is common between a tall human and 'nakhl', but the rest of tall things do not share with 'nakhl' in these attributes.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
The passage begins by addressing an objection: if using the word 'sale' as a metaphor for 'lease' is valid, why is the specific phrase 'sold you the benefits' invalid? The answer distinguishes between the metaphorical use itself (which is valid) and the grammatical addition of the verb to benefits (which is invalid because benefits are non-existent at contract time). The text then explains that even in a direct lease, one cannot say 'I leased you the benefits'; rather, one must say 'I leased you the house' where the house stands in for the benefits. Therefore, the same applies to the metaphorical use of 'sale'. The passage then generalizes: examples like marriage with the word 'gift', divorce with the word 'love', and lease with the word 'sale' are all metaphors, not real cause-effect applications, because the literal cause (e.g., gift) does not produce the same legal effects. The metaphor is one-way (lion for brave, not brave for lion). Finally, it discusses the condition of 'samaa' (hearing from linguists): only the types of relationships need to be attested, not every particular instance. This allows for creative metaphors in eloquence, but some scholars insist on hearing the particulars, using the example of 'nakhl' (palm tree) not being applied to all tall things. The refutation shows that the resemblance must be in the most specific attributes, not just tallness.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical use of a word for something other than its original meaning due to a relationship. In this lesson, it applies to contractual words like 'sale' for lease.
استعارہ (istiʻārah): Metaphor based on resemblance; e.g., 'lion' for a brave man.
حقیقت (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning of a word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the phrase 'I sold you the benefits of this house' invalid as a lease contract?
A) Because 'sale' cannot be used as a metaphor for 'lease'.
B) Because benefits are non-existent at contract time and cannot be the object of addition.
C) Because 'sale' requires a fixed price.
D) Because metaphor is not allowed in contracts.

Key Takeaway
Metaphor in contracts is valid only if one-way and the grammatical construction does not add to non-existent objects like benefits.

Condition of Samāʻ for Majāz: Type vs Particular
Analyzing the condition of samāʻ for majāz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for samāʻ (hearing) in majāz and evaluate the opposing view that requires hearing particulars.

Core Idea
For a metaphorical relationship to be valid, only the type of relationship needs to be heard from linguists, not every specific instance.

The text addresses a key methodological principle: how much of a metaphor must be attested by Arabic linguists? The Hanafi school holds that hearing the type of relationship is sufficient, allowing for creative metaphors. Some scholars require hearing every particular instance. We will analyze the argument and the example of 'nakhl' (palm tree) to understand the Hanafi position and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
What is the condition for validity of a metaphorical relationship? The text asks whether every particular metaphorical usage must be directly heard from linguists, or only the general type of relationship.
2
State the Hanafi position
Only the type of relationship (نوع العلاقة) needs to be heard from linguists. The particulars (جزئیات) can be created by the speaker as long as they follow the attested type.
3
State the opposing view
Some scholars (عند البعض) require hearing from linguists for every particular instance. They argue that without such hearing, we might incorrectly extend a metaphor to inappropriate cases.
4
Analyze the supporting argument and example of 'nakhl'
The opponents use the example of 'nakhl' (palm tree): it is applied to a tall human but not to every tall thing. They claim that if hearing particulars were not required, we would apply 'nakhl' to all tall things. The Hanafi response is that the resemblance must be in the most specific attributes (اخص صفات), not just a general attribute like tallness.
5
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi argue that the restriction on using 'nakhl' for all tall things is due to the lack of specific shared attributes (e.g., tallness with straightness and death by beheading), not due to the necessity of hearing each particular. Thus, creative metaphors are allowed as long as they are based on a valid type relationship.
