But it is not valid with this wording while ijarah is not correct with these words, so responding to this, it was said: This invalidity due to the mentioned wording is not because the metaphor is corrupt. Rather, the word 'sale' as a metaphor for 'lease' is correct, but the addition of 'sale' to benefits is not correct because the benefit is non-existent at the time of sale. It cannot be a locus of addition, so the addition for the contract of these non-existent benefits is not valid as a locus, even so the addition of the lease contract itself to benefits is not correct. For example, if one says 'I leased to you the benefits of this house for a month for such amount', the lease is not valid. Likewise, the metaphor for it, i.e., the word 'sale', its addition to benefits is also not correct. So the lease is only correct when the lease contract is added to the substance, and in the contract addition, this substance takes the place of the benefit. For example, if one says 'I leased you this house for a month for such amount', and similarly when one relates 'sale' to the substance and says 'I sold you this house for a month for such amount', then the lease is correct. So adding 'sale' to benefits is not correct, just as adding 'lease' to benefits is not correct. Then know that in these examples there is an investigation. Summary: The examples mentioned, i.e., marriage with the word 'gift', divorce with the word 'love', and lease with the word 'sale', the truth is that all these are by way of metaphor. They are not by way of applying the name of the cause to the effect, because the gift is not a cause for the ownership of enjoyment that is established by marriage, and similarly 'sale' is not actually a cause, because with the ownership of enjoyment established by marriage, the authorities of divorce, lian, zihar, etc. are established. Unlike the ownership of enjoyment that comes with the word 'gift' or 'sale' in a transaction, which does not accept these matters. Rather, the application of the word is to something that is completely opposite in meaning, but they share in necessary [aspects], and this is called metaphor. The reverse, i.e., using the effect for the cause is not correct. The reason is that metaphor is one-way, it does not run from both sides, just as the metaphor 'lion' for a brave man is correct, but the metaphor 'brave' for a lion is not correct. As for the example of sale and ownership, it is correct because sale is a cause of ownership, and ownership is the effect of sale. And know that the hearing from linguists is considered regarding the types of relationships, i.e., for the validity of metaphor, hearing only the types of relationships is sufficient, and hearing the particulars of metaphor is not necessary. Because producing wonderful subtle metaphors is part of eloquence, and this can happen when the creation of particular metaphors is done by oneself. If every single particular were heard from linguists, then subtle metaphors could not be produced. And according to some, hearing from linguists is necessary, i.e., for a particular type of relationship, we can only use what the linguists have used, we cannot exceed it. For example, 'nakhl' is applied to a tall human, but it is not applied to any other tall thing. If hearing of particulars were not a condition, then due to resemblance in tallness, we would apply 'nakhl' to everything tall, while this is not the case. We respond that applying one thing to another based on resemblance requires that the resemblance be in the most specific attributes. And 'nakhl' and a tall human share in tallness with straightness and the occurrence of complete death after beheading. This is common between a tall human and 'nakhl', but the rest of tall things do not share with 'nakhl' in these attributes.

Commentary
The passage begins by addressing an objection: if using the word 'sale' as a metaphor for 'lease' is valid, why is the specific phrase 'sold you the benefits' invalid? The answer distinguishes between the metaphorical use itself (which is valid) and the grammatical addition of the verb to benefits (which is invalid because benefits are non-existent at contract time). The text then explains that even in a direct lease, one cannot say 'I leased you the benefits'; rather, one must say 'I leased you the house' where the house stands in for the benefits. Therefore, the same applies to the metaphorical use of 'sale'. The passage then generalizes: examples like marriage with the word 'gift', divorce with the word 'love', and lease with the word 'sale' are all metaphors, not real cause-effect applications, because the literal cause (e.g., gift) does not produce the same legal effects. The metaphor is one-way (lion for brave, not brave for lion). Key Terms
سماع (samāʻ): Hearing from Arabic linguists; condition for validity of a type of metaphorical relationship.
نوع العلاقة (naw' al-ʻalāqah): Type of relationship; the general category of connection between the literal and metaphorical meanings.
جزئیات مجاز (juz'iyyāt al-majāz): Particular instances of a metaphorical relationship.
اخص صفات (akhass ṣifāt): Most specific attributes; the precise characteristics that justify a metaphor.

A) Because the particular usage has not been heard from linguists.
B) Because the resemblance must be in the most specific attributes, which are not present in all tall things.
C) Because 'nakhl' is only a literal word, not a metaphor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that only the type of metaphorical relationship needs to be heard from linguists, not every particular instance, allowing creative metaphors while respecting linguistic foundations.

Critique of the Distinction Between Ism Jins and Ism Mushtaq in Isti'ārah al-Khabar
Critiquing the earlier claim about isti'ārah in khabar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi critique of the claim that isti'ārah in khabar depends on whether the predicate is an ism jins or ism mushtaq, and evaluate the argument that the possibility of literal meaning is not a condition for majāz.

Core Idea
Isti'ārah in khabar does not depend on noun type; impossibility is not a barrier with a proper qarīnah.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement from a Hanafi usul commentary. The author refutes a distinction made by earlier scholars (possibly from the scholars of bayān) regarding when isti'ārah (metaphor) occurs in a predicate (khabar). The earlier claim held that when the predicate is an ism jins like 'Zaidun asadun', it is not isti'ārah because it entails an impossible claim, but when the predicate is an ism mushtaq like 'al-hāl nātiqah', it is isti'ārah. The author shows this distinction is weak on multiple grounds, and that the validity of isti'ārah does not require the possibility of the literal meaning. We will follow the argument and extract the core Hanafi principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the earlier claim
The earlier claim asserts that in 'Zaidun asadun' (Zaid is a lion), the predicate is an ism jins, and to avoid the impossible claim that Zaid is literally a lion, one must assume a tool of simile. In contrast, in 'al-hāl nātiqah' (the state is speaking), the predicate is an ism mushtaq, and no impossibility arises, so it is a genuine isti'ārah.
Focus on the distinction: the type of noun determines whether we need to assume a simile tool.
2
Identify the author's first refutation
The author points out that both statements involve an impossible claim: 'al-hāl nātiqah' is as impossible as 'Zaid is a lion'. The distinction based on intention (qasd) is invalid because any speech containing an impossibility is false regardless of intent.
Key: the impossibility is inherent; intention does not change it.
3
Analyze the second refutation
The author shows that isti'ārah often involves impossible claims (e.g., 'Zaid is the moon'), yet it is accepted because of subtle considerations and the presence of a qarīnah that prevents literal interpretation.
Thus, impossibility does not automatically invalidate isti'ārah.
4
Examine the argument about qalb al-haqā'iq
The author refutes the claim that isti'ārah in ism jins leads to qalb al-haqā'iq (turning impossibility into possibility) while ism mushtaq does not. They argue that 'al-hāl nātiqah' also involves an impossibility, so if it is considered isti'ārah, then the condition of possible literal meaning is not required.
This shows that the earlier distinction collapses.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position is that the validity of isti'ārah does not depend on the type of noun (ism jins vs ism mushtaq) or on the possibility of the literal meaning. Rather, it relies on the presence of a qarīnah and the speaker's intent. Even if the distinction were accepted, the case under discussion involves an ism mushtaq, so isti'ārah would be valid by consensus.
This reinforces the principle that isti'ārah is a type of majāz that does not require the potential for literal truth.

اسم مشتق ہو تو یہ قلب حقائق کہ مستلزم نہیں ہوگا۔ جیسے الحال ناطقہ میں ناطقہ کا استعارہ برائے دالہ قلب حقیقہ کو مستلزم نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کے اندر ایسی وصف کا اثبات للشتی ہے جو حقیقتہ اس کے لئے ثابت نہیں ہے . وعلمنا خبر المبتدا اور ہمارے زیر بحث مسئلہ ہذا اپنی میں خبر اسم مشتق ہے ۔ لان معناہ اسی معنی لفظ اپنی مولود منی تو اس میں حسب ضابطہ استعارہ جاری ہو جائے گا ۔ جیسے الحال ناطقہ میں ہے۔ واعلما تهم يمون الاستعانة یعنی علماء بیان والے استعارہ فی اسمام الاجناس کا نام استعارہ اصلیہ رکھتے ہیں اور استعاره في الافعال وفي الاسماء المشتقة كو استعاره تبعیہ کہتے ہیں کیونکہ اولا استعارہ مشتق من ہیں واقع ہوتا ہے پھر اس کے تبعا مشتقات میں واقع ہوتا ہے۔ يجب ان يعلم آپ کو یہ معلوم رہے کہ اسماء الاجناس اور مشتقات کے مابین فرق ہونے کا جو جواب متن میں دیا گیا ہے۔ یہ صرف اس پر مبنی ہے کہ علماء بیان کے زعم مذکور کو تسلیم کر لیا جائے. اور ان کے کمزور دلائل میں مناقشہ ترک کر دیا جائے . وذلك ان قولھم یعنی جو وہ حضرات کہتے ہیں زید اسد استعارہ نہیں ہے اور رائیت اسدا بر می استعارہ ہے ۔ لیس بقوی یہ ان کی بات قومی نہیں ہے ۔ والفرق الذي ذكرته اور جو یہ فرق پیش کیا گیا ہے کہ ان زیداً است امر مستحیل کا قصاً دعوی ہے لہذا اس استحالہ سے بچنے کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ اداہ تشبیہ مقدر مانا جائے تاکہ تشبیہ ہوکر یہ کلام استحالہ سے خارج ہو کر صحت کی طرف آجائے اور بخلاف رائیت اسرایر می کے کہ اس کے اندر اثبات اسد میری رابی زید قصد ا نہیں ہے بلکہ مقصور اثبات رؤیتہ ہے اور یہ امر ستحیل نہیں ہے لہذا اداة تشبیہ مقدر کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے لہذا یہ استعارہ ہوگا۔ انه فرق واہ یہ فرق کمزور ہے کیونکہ جو کلام مشتمل علی المحال ہو باطل ہے خواہ قصداً ہو یا نہ ہو۔ اس قصداً اور بغیر قصد کا فرق صحیح نہیں ہے ۔ اور دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ استعارہ بسا اوقات دعوی امر مستحیل پر شامل ہوتا ہے لیکن استعارہ کو غلط نہیں کہا جاتا جیسے زمی است و تکلم بدر میں ثالثا یہ کہ استعارہ میں کبھی امر محال کا دعویٰ کر لیا جاتا ہے اس کے اندر کچھ اعراض اور اعتبارات لطیفہ پیش نظر ہوتے ہیں ۔ البتہ قرینہ موجود ہوتا ہے جو ثبوت فی الواقع کے ارادہ سے مانع ہوتا ہے۔

وما ذكر بعد ذلك ان في اسماء الاجناس آلم اور اس کے بعد جو انھوں نے ذکر کیا ہے کہ اسماء اجناس اگر خبر مبتدا ہوں تو ایسی خبر میں استعارہ جاری نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر خبر اسماء مشتقہ میں سے ہو تو پھر استعارہ جاری ہوگا۔ یہ پہلے سے بھی اضعف ہے اور جو انھوں نے فرق پیش کیا ہے کہ اول میں یعنی اسماء اجناس میں استعارہ قلب حقائق کی طرف مفضی ہو گا ۔ اور ثانی یعنی مشتقات میں نہیں ہو گا اون من نسبح العنكبوت لان قولهم الحال ناطقة کیونکہ یہ قول الحال ناطقہ استحالہ میں زید اسد سے کم نہیں ہے کیونکہ نطق حال ایسے ہی مستحیل ہے جیسے اسدی مان مستحیل ہے خواہ اس کا نام قلب حقائق رکھیں یا نہ رکھیں اس سے استعمالہ میں کوئی فرق نہیں آتا اور محققین کے نزدیک انقلاب حقائق اس کو کہتے ہیں کہ واجب اور ممکن و ممتنع میں کوئی ایک دوسرے کی طرف تبدیل ہو جاتے اور اس میں کوئی شک نہیں کہ حال کا ناطق ہونا ممتنعات میں سے ہے پھر جب آپ ثبوت نطق برائے حال تسلیم کرتے ہیں تو یہ اس کا امکان ہوا قلب حقائق یعنی امکان الممتنع تو مشتقات میں بھی لازم آتا ہے۔ فما الذي اوجب پھر وہ کیا شئی جو واجب قرار دیتی ہے کہ ان احدهما اى الحال ناطقہ استعارہ ہے۔ والآخرای زیر اسد استعارہ نہیں ہے وہ کون سا فرق ہے۔ فان قولهم الحال ناطقہ پس جب ان کا یہ قول بالاتفاق ہے کہ الحال ناطقہ استعارہ ہے تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ معنی حقیقی کا امکان مجاز کی صحت کے لئے شرط نہیں ہے ۔ جیسے استعارہ کی صحت کیلئے شرط نہیں ہے کیونکہ استعارہ بھی تو از قسم مجاز ہے. وعلی تقدیر تسلیم الفرق اگر مشتقات اسماء اجناس کے اندر ان کے فرق کو تسلیم کر کبھی لیا جائے تو ہذا انہی کے اندر صحۃ استعارہ پر کوئی اثر نہیں پڑا کیونکہ ہذا ابنی از قبیل مشتقات ہے لہذا اس میں تو بالاتفاق استعارہ صحیح ہونا چاہئے بغیر اس شرط کے کہ معنی حقیقی کا امکان ہو کیونکہ یہ استعارہ مشتقات میں شرط نہیں ہے۔
If the predicate is a derived noun, then this does not entail qalb al-haqa'iq. For example, in 'al-hal natiqa', the isti'ara of 'natiqa' for 'dalla' does not entail qalb al-haqiqa, but rather it affirms for it a description that is not actually true for it. And we know the khabar of mubtada in our subject under discussion is a derived noun, because its meaning is the meaning of 'mawlud minni'. So according to the rule, isti'ara would apply, as in 'al-hal natiqa'. The scholars of bayan call isti'ara in nouns of genus 'original isti'ara' and isti'ara in verbs and derived nouns 'subsidiary isti'ara', because first isti'ara occurs in the root, then following it in derivatives. It should be known that the answer given in the text regarding the difference between nouns of genus and derivatives is based on accepting the claim of the scholars of bayan and not disputing their weak arguments. That is because their statement that 'Zaid is a lion' is not isti'ara, and 'I saw a lion shooting' is isti'ara, is not strong. The distinction they mentioned that in 'Zaid is a lion' there is a claim of an impossible matter, so to avoid this impossibility it is necessary to assume a tool of simile so that it becomes a simile and leaves impossibility, unlike 'I saw a lion shooting' where there is no claim of affirming lion-ness for Zaid, but rather the purpose is to affirm the seeing, and this is not impossible, so there is no need to assume a tool of simile, hence it is isti'ara. This distinction is weak because any speech that contains an impossibility is false, whether intended or not. The distinction between intended and unintended is invalid. Second, isti'ara sometimes includes a claim of an impossible matter, but isti'ara is not considered incorrect, like 'Zaid is the moon' and 'he spoke about the full moon' etc. Third, sometimes in isti'ara a claim of an impossible matter is made, but it has subtle considerations and intentions, and there is a qarina that prevents intending actual existence. And what they mentioned after that that in nouns of genus if they are khabar of mubtada, then isti'ara does not apply, but if the khabar is from derived nouns, then isti'ara applies — this is even weaker. The distinction they made that in the first (nouns of genus) isti'ara leads to qalb al-haqa'iq, and in the second (derived nouns) it does not — is not correct, because their statement 'al-hal natiqa' is not less impossible than 'Zaid is a lion', because 'natiqa' for 'hal' is as impossible as being a lion for Zaid, whether one calls it qalb al-haqa'iq or not, it makes no difference in isti'ara. According to the verifiers, qalb al-haqa'iq is when something necessary or possible turns into something impossible, and there is no doubt that 'hal' being natiqa is among the impossibilities. Then if you affirm the existence of natiqa for 'hal', this entails its possibility, i.e., the possibility of the impossible, which is qalb al-haqa'iq, and this also applies to derivatives. So what makes it obligatory that one (al-hal natiqa) is isti'ara and the other (Zaid is a lion) is not isti'ara? What distinction is there? Then if their statement 'al-hal natiqa' is unanimously considered isti'ara, it shows that the possibility of the real meaning is not a condition for the validity of majaz, nor for the validity of isti'ara because isti'ara is also a type of majaz. And assuming the acceptance of the distinction between derivatives and nouns of genus, then this (i.e., 'hadha ibn...') is of the category of derivatives, so it should be unanimously valid as isti'ara without the condition of the possibility of the real meaning, because this condition is not required for isti'ara in derivatives.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah) of a previous claim found in the matn regarding the occurrence of isti'ārah (metaphor) in khabar (predicate) when the predicate is an ism mushtaq (derived noun) versus an ism jins (noun of genus). The author of the sharh (commentary) is arguing against a distinction made by earlier scholars (perhaps from the matn or from the scholars of bayān). The earlier claim stated that when the khabar is an ism jins like 'Zaidun asadun', it is not isti'ārah because it entails a claim of an impossible reality (i.e., Zaid actually being a lion), so to salvage the speech, one must assume a tool of tashbīh (simile). In contrast, when the khabar is an ism mushtaq like 'al-hāl nātiqah', it is considered isti'ārah because it does not entail an impossible claim. The author refutes this by showing that both types involve impossible claims: 'al-hāl nātiqah' (the state speaking) is equally impossible as 'Zaid is a lion'. Furthermore, the author argues that the distinction based on qasd (intention) is invalid because any speech containing an impossibility is false regardless of intent. Additionally, isti'ārah itself often involves impossible claims (e.g., 'Zaid is the moon'), yet it is accepted because of subtle considerations and the presence of a qarīnah (contextual indicator) that prevents literal interpretation. The author also points out that the condition of possibility of real meaning is not required for the validity of majāz (metaphorical usage), as seen from the unanimous acceptance of 'al-hāl nātiqah' as isti'ārah. Finally, even if the distinction between ism jins and ism mushtaq were accepted, the author's own example (likely 'hādhā ibnī' or similar) falls under ism mushtaq, so isti'ārah should be valid by consensus without needing the possibility condition. This passage reinforces a key Hanafi usul principle: the validity of isti'ārah does not depend on the type of noun or the possibility of literal meaning; rather, it relies on the presence of a qarīnah and the speaker's intent.

Key Terms
استعارہ (isti'ārah): Metaphorical usage of a word in a sense other than its original meaning with a qarīna that prevents literal interpretation.
اسم جنس (ism jins): A noun that denotes a genus or category, not derived from a verb.
اسم مشتق (ism mushtaq): A derived noun that comes from a verbal root, such as fa'il or maf'ul.
قلب حقائق (qalb al-haqā'iq): Transforming a necessary or possible thing into an impossibility, which is considered problematic in metaphorical usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the distinction between ism jins and ism mushtaq in the occurrence of isti'ārah in khabar?
A) Because both involve an impossible claim, and the distinction based on intention is invalid.
B) Because derived nouns cannot be used in isti'ārah.
C) Because nouns of genus are always literal.
D) Because the earlier scholars were not qualified.

Key Takeaway
Isti'ārah in khabar is valid regardless of noun type; impossibility is not a barrier when a qarīnah is present.

The Rule: A Single Word Cannot Carry Both Haqiqah and Majaz
Explaining a core usul rule with branches and responses to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a single word cannot intend both haqiqah and majaz simultaneously, and apply it to derive rulings in fiqh cases.

Core Idea
A single word can only convey one meaning at a time: either its literal or figurative sense, never both.

This lesson examines a foundational usul principle: a word cannot be used to convey both its original (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings in a single utterance. The rule is rooted in the hierarchy of language: haqiqah is the original and is given precedence. Through examples from wills, criminal law, and oaths, we see how the rule operates and how apparent conflicts are resolved using the concept of 'umum al-majaz.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish Haqiqah and Majaz
Identify the two types of word usage: haqiqah (literal, original meaning) and majaz (figurative, derived meaning). Understand that haqiqah is the basis (matbu') and majaz is subsidiary (tabi').
For example, 'khamr' literally means grape-based intoxicant; it is used figuratively for other intoxicants.
2
State the Rule and Its Justification
State: لا يراد من اللفظ الواحد معناه الحقيقي والمجازي معا. The justification is that the original (haqiqah) is preferred over the subsidiary (majaz), so both cannot be intended together.
This is a logical priority: the stronger (original) cannot coexist with the weaker (derived) in a single intention.
3
Apply the Rule to Examples
Work through the examples: will for 'mawali' (only mu'taq inherits), hadd for khamr (only grape wine triggers hadd unconditionally), Quranic 'lamastum' (figurative meaning of intercourse intended, so literal touch excluded).
In each case, one meaning is intended, and the other is excluded.
4
Handle Apparent Conflicts with 'Umum al-Majaz
When a word seems to combine meanings (e.g., oath not to place foot), the Hanafi school resolves it through 'umum al-majaz: the figurative meaning is intended generally, including the literal. This is not a true combination.
The literal meaning is abandoned (mahjur) in such contexts.
5
Compare with Shafi'i View on Generality of Majaz
Note the dispute: Shafi'is deny generality to majaz, limiting it to the specific figurative sense. Hanafis affirm generality. Trace the practical difference using the sa' hadith.
The thamarah al-khilaf: does the prohibition apply only to food or to all things measured by sa'?

ھے تو مجاز جو حقیقت جیسا ایک نوع کلام ہے تو اس میں بھی عموم ہونا چاہتے۔ دھونی کلام اللہ تعالیٰ کثیر یہ متکلم سے استعمال مجاز میں ضرورۃ اور مجبوری کی نفی کے لئے دلیل ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کی کلام مقدس میں استعمال مجاز بالکثرة ہے۔ جیسے اللہ تعالیٰ کا قول پیریدان نقض برید یہاں حقیقی معنی میں نہیں ہے کیونکہ ارادہ وصف جدار نہیں ہو سکتا اور اسی طرح لماطعی المامہ میں طغی اپنے حقیقی معنی میں مستعمل نہیں ہے ۔ اور اللہ تعالٰی عجز و ضرورت سے متعالی اور مقدس ہے۔ نظيره قولة عليه السلام لا تبيعو الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعین تو صاع میں اطلاق محل اور ارادہ حال کے طور پر چونکہ مجاز اطعام مراد ہے لہذا امام شافعی کے نزدیک یہ غیر طعام کو شامل نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مجازہ میں ان کے نزدیک عموم نہیں ہے۔ اور ہمارے نزدیک جوشتی صاع کے ساتھ کیل کی جاتی ہے خواہ مطعوم ہو یا بغیر مطعوم یہ سب کو شامل ہوگا ۔ مسئلة لا يراد من اللفظ الواحد معناه الحقيقي والمجازى معا الخ ایک لفظ سے معنی حقیقی اور معنی مجازی دونوں معابیک وقت مراد نہیں ہو سکتے ۔ درحجان المتبوع على التابع کیونکہ حقیقت متبوع واصل ہے اور مجاز تابع و فرع ہے اور متبوع کو ترجیح علی التابع حاصل ہے لہذا متبوع اور راج کے ساتھ تابع اور مرجوع کا ارادہ کیسے کیا جاسکتا ہے۔ بہر حال ایک لفظ سے ایک معنی مراد ہو گا خواہ حقیقی ہو یا مجازی دونوں مجتمعا مراد نہیں ہو سکتے۔ فلا يستحق معتق المعتق الخ یہ اس مسئلہ کی تفریعات ہیں۔ ایک شخص کا معتق یعنی ازاد کرده شده غلام موجود ہے اور معتق المعتق بھی یعنی آزاد کردہ شدہ کا ازاد کردہ ۔ اب ایک شخص نے اسی شخص مذکور کے موالی کیلئے وصیت کی تو معتق مستحق ہوگا اور عتق المعتق مستحق نہیں ہوگا کیوں کہ لفظ مولیٰ حقیقہ ہے مولیٰ اسفل یعنی معتق میں اور معشق المعتق میں مجازہ ہے چونکہ لفظ حقیقی معنی میں بدرجہ مقبوع واصل ہے لہذا اسی معنی حقیقی پر محمول ہوگا ۔ اور معتق المعتق میں مجاز تھا۔ تو جب حقیقہ۔ مراد ہو چکی ہے تو مجازی معنی مراد نہیں لیا جا سکتا لہذا معتق المعتق مستحق نہیں ہوگا ولا بواد غير الخمر به تفریح ثانی ہے یعنی رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے قول من شرب الخمر فاجلدوه یعنی یہاں خمر کا حقیقی معنی یعنی الماء التي من العنب اذا اشتد بالاجماع مراد ہے تو اس سے خمر کے علاوہ باقی مسکرات مراد نہیں ہو سکتے کیونکہ ان پر اطلاق خمر بطور مجاز کے ہے بعلاقة مشابهة في مخامرة العقل البتہ خمر کے علاوہ باقی مسکرات میں حد بشرط السکر جاری ہوگی جس کے لئے دلیل اجتماع ہے اور حقیقی خمر میں سکر وغیرہ کی کوئی شرط نہیں ہے ہر صورت میں اس حدیث مذکور کی رو سے حد قائم ہوگی ۔ واد المس باليد بقوله تعالى اولا مستم النساء یعنی اس قول میں لامستم کا مجازی معنی یعنی جماع مراد بالا جماع ہو چکا ہے اب حقیقی معنی یعنی لمس باليد مراد نہیں ہو سکتا ورنہ تو جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز لازم آئیگی جو کہ صحیح نہیں ہے ۔ یہ تفریحات ثلثہ ہیں تفریح اول اور ثانی میں حقیقہ۔ مراد ہو چکی ہے لہذا مجاز مراد نہیں ہو سکتا پہلی تفریح میں تحقق حقیقت نی نسبتہ اوصی الی موالیہ میں ہے اور ثانی میں تحقیق حقیقت فی المفرد ہے یعنی فی الخمر اور ثالث میں مجاز متحقق ہے لہذا حقیقت مراد نہیں ہو سکتی۔ وكذا اذا اوصى لاولاد فلان اسی طرح جب کوئی شخص اولا د فلاں کے لئے وصیت کرتا ہے تو وصیت ابناء فلاں کے لئے جاری ہوگی اور فلاں کے ابناء الابناءہ کو کچن میں ملے گا کیونکہ حقیقی معنی یعنی انسامہ فلاں جب مراد ہے تو مجاز یعنی ابناء الا بناء مراد نہیں ہو سکتے ۔ اما دخول بني البنين في الامان الخ یہ جواب ہے اس اثر کال کا کہ جب غیر مسلم لوگ کہتے ہیں آمنونا علی ابنار نا تو اس میں احناف کہتے ہیں کہ امان میں ابناء کے ساتھ ابناء الابنا بھی داخل ہو جائیں گے تو یہ جمع بین الحقیقۃ والمجاز حقیقی معنی یہ ہے کہ دار اس کی مملوکہ ہو لیکن اس صورت مسئلہ میں خواہ اس کی دار مملوکہ میں داخل ہو یا عاریتہ واجدہ والی دار ہو جس میں داخل ہو حانث ہو جائیگا تو یہ جمع بین الحقیقۃ والمجاز ہے۔ تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ دار فلان سے مراد نسبتہ سکنی ہے کیونکہ ذات دار کے ساتھ عداوت نہیں ہوتی بلکہ ساکنین کی وجہ سے ہوتی ہے تو مراد یہ ہوگی کہ جو دار اس شخص کی طرف
Then majaz, being similar to haqiqah as a type of speech, should also have generality. The argument that speech of Allah, the Exalted, is free from need of using majaz for necessity or compulsion is evidence that in the sacred speech of Allah, the Exalted, the use of majaz is abundant. Like Allah's saying 'yuridu an yanquda' [He wants to break down] – here it is not in its literal sense because will cannot be attributed to a wall. Similarly, 'lama tagha al-ma' [when the water rose] – 'tagha' is not used in its literal sense. And Allah, Exalted, is far removed from incapacity and need. Its example is his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor a sa' for two sa's.' Regarding sa', because it is applied to the place (container) and intended for the current situation, since it is majaz for food (that which is eaten), according to Imam Shafi'i it will not include what is not food because, according to him, majaz does not have generality. And according to us, 'sa'' is a measure, whether it is food or non-food, it will include all. The issue: A single word cannot be intended to convey both its literal and figurative meanings simultaneously. Due to the preference of the original over the derived, because haqiqah is the original and basis, and majaz is derived and subsidiary, and the original has preference over the derived; thus how can the derived and inferior be intended along with the original and superior? In any case, one word will convey one meaning, whether literal or figurative; both cannot be intended together. So (his saying): 'The manumitted of the manumitted is not entitled' – these are branches of this issue. A person's manumitted slave exists and also the manumitted of that manumitted. Now a person makes a will for the mawali of this mentioned person; the manumitted will be entitled and the manumitted of the manumitted will not be entitled because the word mawla is literal for the lower mawla (the manumitted) and majaz for the manumitted of the manumitted. Since the word is in its literal meaning to the degree of being original and basis, it is construed upon that literal meaning. And in the manumitted of the manumitted it was majaz. So when the literal meaning is intended, the figurative meaning cannot be intended, hence the manumitted of the manumitted is not entitled. And the second branch: his saying, peace be upon him, 'Whoever drinks khamr, flog him' – here the literal meaning of khamr, i.e., the juice from grapes when it intoxicates, is intended by consensus, so from this, other intoxicants cannot be intended because the application of khamr to them is by way of majaz with a relationship of similarity in veiling the intellect. However, for intoxicants other than khamr, the hadd penalty will be applied conditional on intoxication, for which there is a proof from consensus. And for literal khamr, there is no condition of intoxication; in every case the hadd is established according to the mentioned hadith. And (the third branch): the touching with the hand – by His saying, 'Aw lamastumu al-nisa' [or you have touched women] – in this saying, the figurative meaning of lamastum, i.e., sexual intercourse, is already intended, so the literal meaning, i.e., touching with the hand, cannot be intended; otherwise, combining haqiqah and majaz would be necessary, which is not correct. These are three branches. In the first and second branches, the literal meaning is already intended, therefore the figurative cannot be intended. In the first branch, the literal meaning is realized in the relation of a will to his mawali, and in the second, the literal meaning is realized in the single word, i.e., in khamr. And in the third branch, the figurative is realized, so the literal cannot be intended. Similarly, when a person makes a will for 'the children of so-and-so', the will applies to the sons of so-and-so, and the grandsons receive nothing because when the literal meaning, i.e., the sons of so-and-so, is intended, the figurative, i.e., the grandsons, cannot be intended. As for the inclusion of grandsons in aman (safe conduct) – this is the answer to the objection: when non-Muslims say, 'Grant us aman over our sons,' the Hanafis say that in aman, along with sons, grandsons will also be included. So this would be combining haqiqah and majaz. One answer given is that in this issue there are two narrations: one narration is that grandsons are not included in aman; then there is no problem. However, the other narration is that they will be included. The answer to the objection on this is that this issue is about sparing blood, and sparing blood is established on the basis of the slightest doubt. Since here too there is a doubt that the word 'banun' (sons) is also applied to all descendants, like in 'Bani Adam' and 'Bani Hashim', so on the basis of this doubt, all descendants are included. Then (the author says): 'And avoid combining both' – these are several issues regarding which it was thought that there is combining of haqiqah and majaz. He mentions them and wants to answer them. The first issue: 'If a person swears that he will not place his foot in the house of so-and-so, then if he enters barefoot, or wearing shoes, or on a mount, he will be an oath-breaker.' Entering barefoot is the literal meaning, i.e., entering with foot uncovered, and the rest are figurative. So here there is combining of haqiqah and majaz. The answer is: no combining, because this is from the chapter of 'umum al-majaz (generality of figurative meaning), i.e., a general figurative meaning is intended, which includes the literal meaning. 'And we construe it upon the figurative meaning' – the reason is that the literal meaning has become abandoned. Its literal meaning is that he places his two feet inside the house and the rest of the body outside the house; this literal meaning is not intended but is abandoned. So in custom, the phrase 'he will not place his foot' has become a metonym for 'he will not enter the house', and entering is a general meaning, whether barefoot, shod, or riding. Similarly, from the chapter of 'umum al-majaz is his saying: 'He does not enter the house of so-and-so' – meaning the relationship of dwelling. When a person swears that he will not enter the house of so-and-so, the literal meaning of 'house of so-and-so' is that the house is his owned property. But in this issue, whether he enters his owned house or a borrowed or rented house, he breaks the oath. So this would be combining haqiqah and majaz. The answer is that 'house of so-and-so' is intended to mean the relationship of dwelling, because enmity is not towards the house itself but towards the dwellers. So the intended meaning is that the house that belongs to this person
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a key usul rule: a single word cannot be intended to convey both its literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings at the same time. The author begins by discussing majaz in general, noting that Allah's speech uses majaz abundantly (e.g., 'yuridu an yanquda' and 'lama tagha'), but this does not imply necessity or incapacity in Allah. The rule is then stated clearly: لا يراد من اللفظ الواحد معناه الحقيقي والمجازى معا. The reasoning is that haqiqah is the original (matbu') and majaz is subsidiary (tabi'); the original is given preference, so both cannot be intended together. The author then provides several furu' (branches) to illustrate the rule. First, in a will: if someone makes a will for 'his mawali', only the direct freed slave (mu'taq) inherits, not the mu'taq al-mu'taq, because the word mawla is literal for the lower mawla and majaz for the higher. Second, in the hadith on khamr: the literal meaning of khamr (grape-based intoxicant) is intended, so other intoxicants are not included by this hadith; they require intoxication as a condition for the hadd. Third, in the Quranic verse 'aw lamastumu al-nisa': the figurative meaning (sexual intercourse) is already intended, so the literal meaning (touching with hand) cannot be intended. The text then addresses a potential objection regarding the inclusion of grandsons in aman, answering that in that issue, the doubt-based leniency of sparing blood allows inclusion, not because of combining haqiqah and majaz but due to a different principle (i.e., shubhah). Finally, the author discusses issues where apparent combining is resolved by 'umum al-majaz (general figurative meaning), such as an oath not to place one's foot in a house, where the literal meaning (barefoot placement) is abandoned and the figurative meaning (any entry) is intended. Similarly, an oath not to enter the house of someone is interpreted based on dwelling relationship, not ownership. Throughout, the passage maintains the Hanafi position that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined, and apparent conflicts are resolved through other usul principles.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): The original, literal meaning of a word as it was first coined.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original due to a relationship, with the original meaning being abandoned in that usage.
عموم المجاز (umum al-majaz): A general figurative meaning that includes the literal meaning and other possibilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule discussed, if a person swears 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' and then enters wearing shoes, is he an oath-breaker?
A) No, because the literal meaning is barefoot entry.
B) Yes, because the figurative meaning (any entry) is intended due to 'umum al-majaz.
C) Yes, because the literal meaning includes shoe entry.
D) No, because the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A single word can only convey one meaning at a time—either haqiqah or majaz—and apparent combinations are resolved through principles like 'umum al-majaz.

Preference Between Haqiqah and Well-Known Majaz
Dispute over preferring literal vs. well-known metaphorical meaning in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for preferring literal or well-known metaphorical meaning when both are present, and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa prioritizes literal meaning; Sahibayn prioritize well-known metaphorical meaning.

This lesson examines a key dispute within the Hanafi school: when a word is used in its literal sense but its metaphorical meaning is commonly known, which should prevail? We will explore the principles behind each position and their practical consequences through the example of an oath concerning wheat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine when this dispute arises: when a word is used in its literal meaning (haqiqah) but also has a well-known metaphorical meaning (majaz muta'arif).
Key condition: the haqiqah is actively used, not abandoned.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa prefers the literal meaning because the original (al-asl) is not abandoned without necessity (darura). The principle: al-asl la yutrak illa bi-darura.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) prefer the well-known metaphorical meaning because it is what speakers commonly understand and intend.
4
Apply to the wheat oath example
In an oath not to eat from wheat: Abu Hanifa says only literal consumption (chewing grains) violates; Sahibayn say any consumption (including bread) violates as the well-known majaz includes inside of the wheat.
Practical consequence: the same oath can be interpreted differently.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is whether the oath is violated by a derivative form of the item. Both positions are considered valid within the Hanafi school.
No definitive tarjih is given in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاما اذا كانت الحقيقة مستعملة يعنى جب حقیقہ مستعمل ہو اور اس کا مجاز متعارف ہو تو ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک معنی حقیقی اولی ہو گا ۔ لان الاصل لا يترك لو یعنی اصل بغیر ضرورۃ کے متروک نہیں ہو سکتا تو جب حقیقہ مستعمل ہے تو اس اصل کو ترک کر کے رجوع الی المجانہ جو کہ فرع ہے نہیں ہو سکتا ۔ وعندهما اور صاحبین کے نزدیک معنی مجازی جو متعارف ہے وہی اولی ہے۔ نظير و لا يا كل من هذه المحنطة الا چونکہ گندم کے دانے چبا کر کھانا مستعمل ہے جو کہ حقیقت ہے اور مجاز متعارف اکل باطنہا ہے یعنی جو اس کے مافیہا سے حاصل ہو تو امام کے نزدیک قضم یعنی دانے چبا کر کھانے سے حانث ہو گا اور اس کی روٹی کھانے سے جانت نہیں ہوگا صاحبین کے نزدیک اکل ما فیہا سے حانث ہو جائے گا جس صورت میں ہو۔ مسئلة وقد يتعد المعنى الحقيقي کبھی معنی حقیقی اور معنی مجازی دونوں معا متعذر ہو جاتے ہیں تو اس وقت یہ کلام لغو سو جائے گی ۔ کقول لمراته الام جیسے کوئی شخص اپنی بیوی کو کہتا ہے اور یہ بیوی خاوند سے عمر میں بڑی ہے یا ہے تو گھر میں چھوٹی لیکن معروف النسب ہے کہ لوگ جانتے ہیں اور مشہور ہے کہ یہ فلاں شخص کی بیٹی ہے جو خاوند کا غیر ہے تو خاوند ایسی بیوی کو خواہ بڑی ہو یا معروفة النسب اس کے متعلق کہتا ہے بندہ بنتی ، تو یہاں نہ معنی حقیقی ہو سکتا ہے نہ مجازی اما الحقية یعنی معنی حقیقی پہلی صورت میں جبکہ بیوی خاوند سے عمر میں بڑی ہے تو بالکل ظاہر ہے کہ حقیقہ یہ اس کی بیٹی نہیں ہو سکتی . وفی الثانی یعنی صغیرہ معروفة النسب میں اس لئے کہ معنی حقیقی اما یثبت مطلقا یعنی اس کے حق میں بھی اور اس کے حق میں بھی جس سے اس عورت کا نسب مشتہر ہے یعنی اس خاوند کا دعوی نسب اس کے حق میں اور اس شخص کے حق میں جس سے عورت کا نسب مشتھر ہے دونوں کے حق میں شرعاً معتبر ہوبان یثبت یہ دونوں کے حق میں معتبر ہونے کی توضیح ہے یعنی اس خاوند مقر سے اس عورت کا نسب ثابت ہو جائے اور ممن اشتھر منہ النسب سے منفی ہو جائے ولا يمكن هذا اور یہ نا ممکن ہے کہ خاوند سے اس کا نسب ثابت ہو اور ممن اشتھر سے منفی ہو لانه يثبت ممن اشتهر منه کيونکه مشهورة
But when the literal meaning is used, i.e. when the literal meaning is used and its metaphorical meaning is well-known, then according to Abu Hanifa the real meaning is preferable. Because the original is not abandoned without necessity. So when the literal meaning is used, one cannot abandon this original and resort to the metaphorical which is secondary. And according to them (the two companions), the metaphorical meaning that is well-known is preferable. Example: 'He should not eat from this wheat'. Since eating the grains by chewing is used, which is the literal meaning, and the well-known metaphorical is eating its inside, i.e. what is obtained from it, then according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), if he chews the grains, he becomes liable; but if he eats its bread, he does not become liable. According to the two companions, eating what is inside it makes him liable in whichever form. Issue: Sometimes both the literal and metaphorical meanings become impossible together, then this speech becomes void. Like saying to his wife 'Umm'? For example, a person says to his wife, and this wife is older than the husband in age, or is younger in the house but well-known in lineage, people know and it is famous that she is the daughter of so-and-so, who is not the husband. So the husband, to such a wife, whether older or well-known lineage, says 'You are my daughter'. Here neither the literal meaning nor the metaphorical is possible. For the literal meaning, in the first case when the wife is older than the husband, it is absolutely obvious that she cannot be his daughter literally. And in the second case, i.e. a minor with well-known lineage, because the literal meaning would be established absolutely, i.e. in relation to him and in relation to the one from whom the woman's lineage is famous. That is, the husband's claim of lineage in relation to him and in relation to the person from whom the woman's lineage is famous, both are legally valid. This is the explanation that both are valid: that the lineage of this woman is established from this husband who is the confessor, and it is negated from the one from whom the lineage is famous. And this is impossible, that her lineage is established from the husband and negated from the famous one, because it is established from the one from whom it is famous.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage discusses a key Hanafi usul rule: when a word is used in its literal meaning (haqiqah) but its metaphorical meaning (majaz) is also well-known (muta'arif), which should be preferred? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the literal meaning is preferable because the principle (al-asl) is to stick to the literal meaning unless there is a necessity (darura) to abandon it. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the well-known metaphorical meaning is preferable, as it is more commonly understood. The example is an oath not to eat wheat: Abu Hanifa says eating the grains (the literal meaning) violates the oath, but eating bread (metaphorical meaning) does not; the Sahibayn say eating anything derived from it violates the oath. The passage then presents a separate issue (mas'ala): where both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible. An example is a husband calling his wife 'my daughter' (binti). If the wife is older than him, literal impossibility is obvious. If she is younger but known as another man's daughter, both literal and metaphorical fail because the literal would establish lineage from the husband and negate it from the real father, which is impossible; metaphorical is also not valid as it would imply false lineage. Thus the speech becomes لغو (void) and has no legal effect.

Key Terms
مجاز متعارف (majaz muta'arif): Metaphorical meaning that is well-known and commonly used, such that it is readily understood by native speakers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sahibayn, if a man swears 'I will not eat from this cow' (la a'kulu min hadhihi al-baqarah) and then drinks milk from that cow, does he break his oath?
A) No, because milk is not meat.
B) Yes, because the well-known majaz of 'eat from the cow' includes dairy products.
C) No, because the milk is not directly from the cow's flesh.
D) Yes, only if he intends to include milk in his oath.

Key Takeaway
When haqiqah is used but majaz is well-known, Abu Hanifa prefers haqiqah while Sahibayn prefer majaz; both are valid Hanafi views.

When Both Haqiqah and Majaz Are Impossible: The Concept of Laghw
Definition of laghw when haqiqah and majaz are both impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an utterance becomes laghw (void) due to impossibility of both literal and well-known metaphorical meanings, and explain the legal consequence.

Core Idea
If neither literal nor metaphorical meaning is possible, the speech is laghw and has no legal effect.

This lesson addresses a boundary case in usul: what happens when a word is used in a context where both its literal and well-known metaphorical meanings are impossible? The text gives the example of a husband calling his wife 'my daughter', which can neither be literal (since she is not his daughter) nor metaphorical (since the metaphor would imply a false lineage). Such speech is deemed laghw (void) and carries no legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine when both haqiqah and majaz muta'arif are impossible due to factual or legal contradictions.
The impossibility must be absolute; if one meaning is possible, the speech is not laghw.
2
Analyze the example
A husband says to his wife 'You are my daughter' (binti). Two cases: wife older than him (literal impossible, metaphorical also impossible because it would falsely claim lineage), or wife younger but known as another's daughter (literal would establish false lineage; metaphorical also false).
In both, both meanings fail.
3
Understand the reasoning
Literal meaning is impossible because the wife is not biologically his daughter. Metaphorical meaning (e.g., treating her as a daughter) is also impossible because the statement is taken as a claim of lineage, which would be legally conflicting.
The metaphor would require a willing suspension of disbelief, but here it creates a legal contradiction.
4
Apply the ruling
Since neither meaning is possible, the speech is laghw; it does not establish lineage and does not trigger any legal effect (e.g., no prohibition of marriage, no oath violation).
The statement is considered void (batil) in legal terms.
5
Generalize the principle
Any utterance where both haqiqah and the recognized majaz are impossible is laghw. This protects against legal consequences based on impossible interpretations.
Contrast: if one meaning is possible, it must be given effect.

Source Text

But when the literal meaning is used, i.e. when the literal meaning is used and its metaphorical meaning is well-known, then according to Abu Hanifa the real meaning is preferable. Because the original is not abandoned without necessity. So when the literal meaning is used, one cannot abandon this original and resort to the metaphorical which is secondary. And according to them (the two companions), the metaphorical meaning that is well-known is preferable. Example: 'He should not eat from this wheat'. Since eating the grains by chewing is used, which is the literal meaning, and the well-known metaphorical is eating its inside, i.e. what is obtained from it, then according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), if he chews the grains, he becomes liable; but if he eats its bread, he does not become liable. According to the two companions, eating what is inside it makes him liable in whichever form. Issue: Sometimes both the literal and metaphorical meanings become impossible together, then this speech becomes void. Like saying to his wife 'Umm'? For example, a person says to his wife, and this wife is older than the husband in age, or is younger in the house but well-known in lineage, people know and it is famous that she is the daughter of so-and-so, who is not the husband. So the husband, to such a wife, whether older or well-known lineage, says 'You are my daughter'. Here neither the literal meaning nor the metaphorical is possible. For the literal meaning, in the first case when the wife is older than the husband, it is absolutely obvious that she cannot be his daughter literally. And in the second case, i.e. a minor with well-known lineage, because the literal meaning would be established absolutely, i.e. in relation to him and in relation to the one from whom the woman's lineage is famous. That is, the husband's claim of lineage in relation to him and in relation to the person from whom the woman's lineage is famous, both are legally valid. This is the explanation that both are valid: that the lineage of this woman is established from this husband who is the confessor, and it is negated from the one from whom the lineage is famous. And this is impossible, that her lineage is established from the husband and negated from the famous one, because it is established from the one from whom it is famous.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage discusses a key Hanafi usul rule: when a word is used in its literal meaning (haqiqah) but its metaphorical meaning (majaz) is also well-known (muta'arif), which should be preferred? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the literal meaning is preferable because the principle (al-asl) is to stick to the literal meaning unless there is a necessity (darura) to abandon it. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the well-known metaphorical meaning is preferable, as it is more commonly understood. The example is an oath not to eat wheat: Abu Hanifa says eating the grains (the literal meaning) violates the oath, but eating bread (metaphorical meaning) does not; the Sahibayn say eating anything derived from it violates the oath. The passage then presents a separate issue (mas'ala): where both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible. An example is a husband calling his wife 'my daughter' (binti). If the wife is older than him, literal impossibility is obvious. If she is younger but known as another man's daughter, both literal and metaphorical fail because the literal would establish lineage from the husband and negate it from the real father, which is impossible; metaphorical is also not valid as it would imply false lineage. Thus the speech becomes لغو (void) and has no legal effect.

Key Terms
لغو (laghw): Speech that is void because neither literal nor metaphorical meaning is possible; it has no legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man tells his aunt (his mother's sister) 'You are my mother'. According to the Hanafi rule, is this statement laghw?
A) Yes, because she is not his literal mother.
B) Yes, because the well-known metaphorical meaning also fails.
C) No, because the well-known metaphorical meaning (respect, care) is possible.
D) No, because the literal meaning is possible (she is like a mother).

Key Takeaway
If both literal and well-known metaphorical meanings are impossible, the speech is laghw and has no legal effect.

Critical Analysis of Fakhral-Islam's Taqrir on 'You Are My Daughter'
Critical analysis of Fakhral-Islam's taqrir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqah, majaz, and dalalat iltizami, and evaluate the impossibility of establishing nasab or tahrim from an ambiguous utterance in the context of Fakhral-Islam's taqrir.

Core Idea
When haqiqah fails, neither dalalat iltizami nor majaz can save the utterance.

This passage critiques Fakhral-Islam's taqrir on the legal effect of a husband calling his wife 'my daughter'. Fakhral-Islam proposes that the utterance could establish either nasab or tahrim. However, the commentator argues that both literal and figurative meanings are impossible—the literal meaning contradicts Shariah, the metaphorical meaning clashes with marriage rights. We will dissect the reasoning and understand the Hanafi usul principles of haqiqah, majaz, and dalalat iltizami.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning (haqiqah) of the utterance
The literal meaning of 'you are my daughter' is the establishment of nasab (lineage) between the husband and wife as father and daughter.
In Hanafi usul, haqiqah is the default meaning unless there is a valid reason to depart from it.
2
Determine why the literal meaning is impossible
The wife's nasab is already well-known from another person (mashhoor min ghayrihi). Shariah contradicts the husband's claim, making nasab impossible because it would conflict with established lineage.
Shariah's contradiction is considered stronger than the husband's own retraction (a fortiori argument).
3
Examine the two alternative interpretations proposed by Fakhral-Islam
Fakhral-Islam suggests either establishing nasab only in the husband's right (impossible due to Shariah contradiction) or establishing tahrim (prohibition). Tahrim could be via implication (dalalat iltizami) or metaphor (majaz).
The passage distinguishes between tahrim as an implicative meaning of nasab and tahrim as an independent metaphorical meaning.
4
Critique Fakhral-Islam's division based on Hanafi usul
The commentator argues that when the literal meaning fails, implicative indication cannot stand alone—its existence depends on the literal meaning. Metaphorical meaning is also impossible because tahrim contradicts the binding marriage contract (milk al-nikah). Thus, the division is unnecessary, and a clearer taqrir would separate the two failures.
The commentator suggests an improved expression: if tahrim is established, it must be through implication or metaphor; both are impossible for distinct reasons.

سکتا فاقول یہ مصنف فرما ر ہے ہیں کہ فخر الاسلام کے اس قول کی " اما ان يثبت في حق النسب او في حق التحریم" اس کی ضرورت نہیں تھی کیونکہ ان الفاظ کا موضوع لہ ثبوت نسب ہے جب نسب ثابت نہیں ہوتا تو ثبوت تحریم بطریق التزام ممکن ہی نہیں ہے کیونکہ جب اصل ثابت نہیں ہے تو فرع کیسے ثابت ہو فهذا التردید یکون قبیحا کیونکہ اس کا حاصل یہ نکلتا ہے کہ دلالت التزامی بدون المطابقی موجود ہو حالانکہ ایسے نہیں ہو سکتا۔ فالدليل الناقی اس تحریم التزامی کا ناقی یہ نہیں ہے کہ یہ ملک نکاح کے منافی ہے بلکہ دلیل نافی موضوع لہ کا عدم ثبوت ہے فعلم تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ ثبوت تحریم کا طریقہ یہاں صرف مجانہ ہے دلالت الترامی نہیں ہو سکتی اور تحریم کا ثبوت بالمجاز متعذر ہے للمنافاة المذكورة یعنی یہ ملک نکاح کے منافی ہے ۔ ولو ردد بعد الوجه اگر فخر الاسلام اپنے شقوق میں یوں تردید کرتے " ان ثبت التحريم فاما ان يثبت بطريق الالتزام وهو محال لعدم ثبوت الموضوع لہ وھو النسب یہاں مناف لملک النکاح کا لفظ نہ بولتے او بطریق المجاز دھو ايضة محال للمنافاة المذكوره تو یوں عبارت احسن ہو جاتی ۔ علامہ صاحب نے تلویح میں فخر الاسلام کے قول کی توجیہہ کی ہے اسے بھی دیکھ لیا جائے ۔
The lineage is established from the well-known person. Now, from this, only the husband's claim can be negated? Or only his right? That is, this claim is valid only in the right of the husband, i.e., lineage is established from him, and not negated from the well-known person? And that is impossible—to establish only in the husband's right without negating from the well-known person is impossible because the pure Shariah contradicts this husband. Because this lineage has become well-known from another, and legally it is established from him. So the contradiction of the Shariah cannot be of a lesser degree than when a person contradicts himself, i.e., after claiming lineage he says 'I lied and made a false claim.' When that contradiction is valid, then the Shariah's contradiction of this person is a fortiori valid. And lineage can be subject to contradiction and retraction, unlike emancipation—when he says similar words to his slave or slave-girl, he is considered a liar and emancipation does not occur. The response is that it is unlike the issue of truth, which does not admit contradiction and retraction, so emancipation would be established. As for the metaphorical meaning, it is also impossible here; it is tahrim (prohibition), i.e., this wife becomes forbidden to him, because the prohibition established with this word is contrary to the ownership of marriage. Therefore, it cannot be from the rights of marriage. Its explanation: if prohibition is established with this word, then it is either such prohibition that requires the validity of the previous marriage, or such prohibition that does not require validity. The second branch is negated because if to a foreign woman such words are said that are well-known of lineage, these words would be futile and no ruling would be established. When he says to his own wife, a fortiori they would be futile. So it is known that if prohibition is established, it would be that prohibition which requires the validity of the previous marriage, and which is one of the rights of marriage like divorce. And that is also impossible because this word 'binti' indicates a prohibition that requires the invalidity of the established marriage. So how can the prohibition that is one of the rights of marriage be established with this word? Know that Fakhral-Islam's exposition on this point is as follows: The literal meaning, i.e., establishment of lineage, either is established in his right—in the right of the husband acknowledging—so that the woman's lineage is established from him, and her lineage is negated from the one from whom it is well-known. That is impossible. Or it is only in his right, i.e., the meaning is only in the right of the husband, then this meaning is either established in the right of lineage (impossible because Shariah contradicts him, since legally it is established from the well-known person) or in the right of prohibition, i.e., that these words establish prohibition. And that is also impossible because the prohibition established with this word is contrary to the ownership of marriage, as we mentioned. As for the metaphorical meaning, it also cannot be correct; it is tahrim, and the metaphorical meaning is tahrim, and it cannot be because it contradicts marriage. The difference between the first and second prohibition: In Fakhral-Islam's exposition, prohibition is first placed under the literal meaning, and then it is considered as a metaphorical meaning. What is the difference? He says: By the first prohibition which is included under the literal meaning, it is that which is established by implicative indication (dalalat iltizami), because the establishment of lineage necessitates prohibition, and the word's indication of a necessary concomitant is implicative. And implicative cannot exist without the literal meaning. By the second prohibition is meant that which is established metaphorically. For example, the word 'saff' (roof): when its literal meaning is intended, it is by literal indication (dalalat mutabaqi); since the roof implies the wall, its indication of the wall is implicative, and this implicative indication which is subsidiary to the literal meaning is not called metaphor. Rather, metaphor is when the word 'saff' is uttered with the intention of 'wall' and the literal meaning is not intended, because in metaphor the literal meaning cannot be intended. So I say: The author says that Fakhral-Islam's statement 'either it is established in the right of lineage or in the right of prohibition' is unnecessary because the literal meaning of these words is establishment of lineage. When lineage is not established, then establishment of prohibition by way of implication is impossible because the branch cannot exist without the root. Thus such a division is ugly because its result is that implicative indication exists without literal indication, which is impossible. The negating proof of this implicative prohibition is not that it contradicts marriage, but rather the negating proof is the non-establishment of the literal meaning. So it becomes known that the means of establishing prohibition here is only metaphorical, not by implicative indication. And establishment of prohibition by metaphor is impossible due to the mentioned contradiction. If Fakhral-Islam had made the division thus: 'If prohibition is established, then either by way of implication (impossible due to non-establishment of the subject, i.e., lineage), and here the word 'contradiction to marriage ownership' is not said, or by way of metaphor (also impossible due to the mentioned contradiction)' then the expression would be better. The author of al-Talwih has explained Fakhral-Islam's statement; it should also be examined.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of Fakhral-Islam al-Pazdawi's taqrir regarding the legal import of a husband saying to his wife 'you are my daughter.' The commentator (likely Sadr al-Shariah or an annotator) argues that Fakhral-Islam's division of possible interpretations into two—establishing nasab in the husband's right or establishing tahrim—is flawed. The literal meaning (haqiqah) of the phrase is the establishment of nasab. However, since the woman's nasab is already well-established from another person (mashhoor min ghayrihi), Shariah contradicts the husband's claim. Therefore, nasab cannot be established only in the husband's right without negating the established nasab, which is impossible. The alternative of establishing tahrim via implication (dalalat iltizami) also fails because the root meaning (nasab) is absent, so the necessary concomitant cannot stand. And the metaphorical (majaz) interpretation of tahrim is impossible because tahrim contradicts the very essence of marriage (milk al-nikah). The commentator improves Fakhral-Islam's expression by stating that if tahrim is to be established, it can only be through implication or metaphor; both are impossible for their respective reasons—implication fails due to the non-existence of the subject (nasab), metaphor fails due to the contradiction with marriage. The passage also distinguishes between 'implicative indication' (dalalat iltizami) and 'metaphor' (majaz), clarifying that the former is not a type of metaphor but a subsidiary meaning dependent on the literal meaning.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word (here: establishment of nasab).
مجاز (majaz): A figurative meaning used when the literal meaning is impossible (here: tahrim). However, even the figurative meaning is impossible because it contradicts a foundational ruling (milk al-nikah).
دلالة التزامية (dalalat iltizami): An implicative indication where the word implies a necessary concomitant of its literal meaning (e.g., nasab implies tahrim). This meaning is dependent on the existence of the literal meaning.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or parental relationship. In this context, the claim that the wife is the husband's daughter.
تحريم (tahrim): Prohibition of marriage due to blood relationship. Here, if the wife were the husband's daughter, she would be permanently prohibited to him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible to establish tahrim through implication (dalalat iltizami) when a husband says 'you are my daughter' to his wife?
A) Because tahrim can only be established through direct Quranic text.
B) Because the literal meaning (nasab) is not established, so its necessary concomitant cannot exist.
C) Because the wife's lineage is unknown.
D) Because metaphor is always preferred over literal meaning.

Key Takeaway
When the literal meaning of an utterance is impossible, neither its implicative nor its metaphorical meanings can establish a legal ruling.

Critique of Establishing Prohibition via Entailment
Refuting the possibility of tahrim through iltizam in marriage context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in relying on implicative indication (dalala iltizamiyya) to establish a legal ruling when the primary meaning is absent, and explain why metaphor (majaz) is also untenable in this specific case.

Core Idea
Implicative indication fails when primary meaning is absent; metaphor fails when it contradicts a settled rule.

This passage critiques Fakhr al-Islam's attempt to divide the establishment of prohibition (tahrim) into two possibilities: by implication (iltizam) or by metaphor (majaz). The commentator shows that neither works in the context of establishing marriage prohibition through lineage. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate the validity of modes of indication in legal argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposed methods
Fakhr al-Islam posits that if prohibition (tahrim) is to be established, it must be either by implicative indication (dalala iltizamiyya) or by metaphor (majaz). Recognize these two categories as the basis of the critique.
Implicative indication means the word implies another meaning necessarily, e.g., 'lineage' implies 'prohibition of marriage'.
2
Understand why implication fails
The commentator argues that implicative indication cannot exist without the primary meaning. Here, the primary meaning is the affirmation of lineage (thubut al-nasab). If lineage is not established, prohibition through implication is impossible. The argument that 'prohibition contradicts ownership of marriage' is not the real reason; the real reason is the absence of the primary meaning.
In Hanafi usul, dalala iltizamiyya depends on the primary meaning (dalala mutabiqiyya).
3
Understand why metaphor fails
Metaphor would require using the word to mean something other than its literal meaning. However, if the word were interpreted metaphorically as 'prohibition', it would contradict the established legal principle that marriage ownership excludes prohibition. Thus, metaphor is also impossible.
Metaphor requires a valid relationship and no contradiction with established rulings.
4
Recognize the commentator's improvement
The commentator suggests a better formulation: first reject implication for lack of primary meaning, then reject metaphor for contradiction. This avoids the logical flaw of implying that implicative indication could exist without its foundation.
The improvement makes the argument more precise.
5
Apply the usul principle
In Hanafi usul, the validity of a mode of indication depends on the certainty of its basis. Implicative indication requires a certain primary meaning; metaphor requires no conflict with established texts. This case shows how careful analysis of modes of indication can strengthen or weaken legal arguments.
This principle is applied in evaluating other usul disputes.

Source Text

کیونکہ اس لفظ کے ساتھ جو تحریم ثابت ہوتی ہے وہ ملک نکاح کے منافی ہے کما ذکرنا۔ وأما المجانی اور اس کلام کا معنی مجازی بھی صحیح نہیں ہو سکتا وهو التحريم اور معنی مجازی تحریم ہے اور یہ اس لئے نہیں ہو کیا فرق ہے فرماتے ہیں ان المراد بالاول یعنی تحریم اول جو حقیقی معنی کے تحت درج کی گئی ہے وہ یہ ہے جو ثابت بدلالة التزامی ہے فات ثبوت النسب موجب للحرمة کیونکہ شبوب نسب مستلزم حرمت ہے اور لفظ کی دلالت لازم موضوع لہ پر التزامی ہوتی ہے اور التزامی بدون المطابقی موجود نہیں ہو سکتی یعنی ثبوت نسب کے ساتھ تحریم بطور التزام کے ہوگی ۔ والمراد بالتحريم الثانی اور تحریم ثانی سے مراد وہ تحریم ہے جو بطریق مجاز ایسے نہیں ہو سکتا۔ فالدليل الناقی اس تحریم التزامی کا ناقی یہ نہیں ہے کہ یہ ملک نکاح کے منافی ہے بلکہ دلیل نافی موضوع لہ کا عدم ثبوت ہے فعلم تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ ثبوت تحریم کا طریقہ یہاں صرف مجانہ ہے دلالت الترامی نہیں ہو سکتی اور تحریم کا ثبوت بالمجاز متعذر ہے للمنافاة المذكورة یعنی یہ ملک نکاح کے منافی ہے ۔ ولو ردد بعد الوجه اگر فخر الاسلام اپنے شقوق میں یوں تردید کرتے " ان ثبت التحريم فاما ان يثبت بطريق الالتزام وهو محال لعدم ثبوت الموضوع لہ وھو النسب یہاں مناف لملک النکاح کا لفظ نہ بولتے او بطریق المجاز دھو ايضة محال للمنافاة المذكوره تو یوں عبارت احسن ہو جاتی ۔ علامہ صاحب نے تلویح میں فخر الاسلام کے قول کی توجیہہ کی ہے اسے بھی دیکھ لیا جائے ۔
Refutation multiple because in this matter the Shariah rejects it because from a legal perspective it has been established from what is well-known. Either the right of prohibition or that these words establish prohibition, and that is not possible, and this is also impossible because the prohibition established with this word contradicts the ownership of marriage as we mentioned. As for the metaphorical meaning, the metaphorical meaning of this speech cannot be correct either, and it is prohibition, and the metaphorical meaning is prohibition, and it cannot be because it contradicts the ownership of marriage. And the difference between the first and second prohibition: In Fakhr al-Islam's exposition, prohibition is first included under literal meaning, and then secondly it is considered as metaphorical meaning. What is the difference? He says that the first prohibition under literal meaning is that which is established by implicative indication, because the affirmation of lineage necessitates prohibition, since [؟] lineage entails prohibition, and the word's indication of the necessary implication is implicative, and implicative cannot exist without the primary meaning, meaning that with the affirmation of lineage, prohibition occurs by way of implication. And by the second prohibition is meant that prohibition which is established by way of metaphor, like the word 'saaq' for example. When the word 'saaq' is used and its literal meaning is intended, that is indicative indication; since ceiling entails wall, then its indication of the wall is implicative, and this implicative indication that follows the intended meaning is not called metaphor. Rather it is metaphor when the word 'saaq' is uttered and the wall is intended, and the literal meaning of 'saaq' is not intended, because at the time of metaphor the literal meaning cannot be intended. I say, the author says that Fakhr al-Islam's statement 'either it is established with respect to lineage or with respect to prohibition' was unnecessary, because the intended meaning of these words is affirmation of lineage; when lineage is not established, then establishing prohibition by way of implication is simply impossible, because when the origin is not established, how can the branch be established? So this division is ugly, because its result is that implicative indication exists without the primary meaning, whereas that cannot be. So the negating evidence of this implicative prohibition is not that it contradicts ownership of marriage, but rather the negating evidence is the non-establishment of the intended meaning. So it became known from this that the way of establishing prohibition here is only by metaphor, implicative indication cannot occur, and establishing prohibition by metaphor is impossible due to the mentioned contradiction, i.e., it contradicts ownership of marriage. And if he had divided after the manner, if Fakhr al-Islam in his divisions had said: 'If prohibition is established, then it is either by way of implication, which is impossible due to non-establishment of the intended meaning which is lineage, and here he would not utter the phrase 'contradicting ownership of marriage', or by way of metaphor, which is also impossible due to the mentioned contradiction', then the expression would be better. Allamah Sahib in Talwih has given an interpretation of Fakhr al-Islam's statement; that should also be seen.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a critique of Fakhr al-Islam's argument regarding the establishment of prohibition (tahrim) through the word in question. The commentator argues that Fakhr al-Islam's division of prohibition into that established by implication (iltizam) or by metaphor (majaz) is flawed. The primary meaning of the words is the affirmation of lineage (nasad). If lineage is not proven, prohibition via implication cannot occur because implication depends on the primary meaning. Similarly, metaphor is impossible because it would contradict the ownership of marriage. The commentator suggests a better formulation: first state that implication is impossible due to lack of primary meaning, then that metaphor is impossible due to contradiction. The reference to Al-Talwih indicates a super-commentary that reconciles Fakhr al-Islam's view.

Key Terms
تحريم (tahrim): Legal prohibition, specifically in the context of marriage (prohibition of marrying a woman due to lineage or other causes)
دلالة التزامية (dalala iltizamiyya): Implicative indication: the meaning implied by the primary meaning, e.g., lineage implies prohibition of marriage.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word for a meaning other than its original, with a relationship and a context clue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is it impossible to establish prohibition (tahrim) through implication (iltizam) in this case?
A) Because implication contradicts the ownership of marriage.
B) Because the primary meaning (lineage) is not established.
C) Because the word cannot be used metaphorically.
D) Because Fakhr al-Islam's argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Implicative indication requires a certain primary meaning; without it, the argument is invalid. Metaphor also fails if it contradicts a settled ruling.
