Components and Incentives of Metaphor (Majaz) in Hanafi Usul
Components and incentives of metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five components of metaphor and distinguish between verbal and meaning-based incentives for using metaphor in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Metaphor requires a source, a target, a relationship, a context clue, and a valid incentive to abandon literal meaning.

This passage from Al-Talweeh explains the essential building blocks of metaphor. In Hanafi usul, metaphor is not arbitrary; it has strict conditions. Understanding these components and the reasons (da'i) for using metaphor helps us interpret legal texts where figurative language is employed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five components of metaphor
List the five elements: musta'ar minhu (source), musta'ar lahu (target), musta'ar (borrowed word), 'ilaqah (relationship), and qarinah (context clue).
These are the necessary conditions for a valid metaphor.
2
Understand the role of each component
Describe what each component contributes: the source is the original meaning, the target is the intended meaning, the word is transferred, the relationship links them, and the clue prevents literal interpretation.
The 'ilaqah is often based on resemblance (tashbih).
3
Classify incentives into verbal and meaning-based
Distinguish between lafzi (verbal) incentives like sweetness, poetic meter, or pun, and ma'nawi (meaning-related) incentives like emphasis.
The text states the da'i must exist for a valid metaphor.
4
Apply the framework to the example in the text
Take the phrase 'ra'aytu asadan yarmi' and identify each component and the incentive.
Here, the qarinah is 'yarmi' (shooting), indicating the lion is metaphorical.
5
Connect to usul al-fiqh
Recognize that this analysis is crucial when interpreting ambiguous texts (mutashabihat) in the Quran and Hadith, where metaphor is often used.
Hanafi usul requires a clear qarinah and da'i to shift from literal to metaphorical meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسئلة الداعي الى المجاز اعلم ان المجاز يحتاج مجاز چند اشیا کی طرف محتاج ہوتا ہے۔ المستعار منه يعنى مشبه به وهو الهيكل المخصوص يعني مخصوص صورة وشكل جو حیوان مفترس کی ہے۔ والمستعار له يعني مشبه وهو الانسان الشجاع والمستعار یعنی وہ لفظ جو مشبہ بہ سے بطور عاریتہ لیکر مشبہ کے لئے استعمال کیا گیا ہے ۔ دھو لفظ الاسد والعلاقه يعني وجه تشبيه وهي الشجاعة والقرينة الصادقة یعنی ایسا قرینہ جو معنی حقیقی کے ارادہ سے صارف اور مانع ہو رھی پر می یعنی وہ لفظ پر می ہے اس قول میں رائیت اسد ایر می تیراندازی کرنا یہ صاف قرینہ ہے کہ اسد سے مراد یہاں حیوان مفترس کی ہیکل مخصوص نہیں ہے بلکہ مراد رجل شجاع ہے۔ والامر الداعي الى استعمال المجاز فانك اذا حاولت جب آپ کا قصد یہ ہے کہ آپ ایک شجاع انسان کی رؤیتہ کی خبر دیں تو اصل یہ ہے کہ آپ کہیں رایت شجاعا جب آپ اس کے قائمقام کہتے ہیں رائیت اسد تو ضروری چیز ہے کہ کوئی ایسا امر ہو جو آپ کے لئے داعی ہوا ہے کہ ادائے مطلوب کے اندر جو اصل ہے یعنی حقیقت اس کی استعمال کو ترک کر کے خلاف اصل یعنی مجاز کو استعمال کریں وذالك الداعی اور یہ داعی با لفظی ہوگا یا معنوی ناللفظی لفظی داعی چند اشیا ہیں اختصاص لفظه ای لفظ المجاز بالعذوبته یعنی لفظ مجاز میٹھا اور مانوس ہے فربما يكون لفظ الحقيقة یعنی کبھی یوں ہوتا ہے کہ لفظ حقیقت ایک لفظ رکیک ہے جس سے طبیعہ تنفر کرتی ہے کلفظ الخنفقیق جو بمعنی الجرثی استعمال ہوتا ہے تو ی لفظ رکیک وحشی ہے تو اس کو چھوڑ کر لفظ اسر استعمال کریں گے جو اس معنی میں مجاز ہے کیونکہ یہ نسبت حقیقہ مذکورہ کے عذب اور مانوس ہے۔ او صلاحية الشعر يعني جب لفظ حقیقہ استعمال کرتے ہیں تو شعر کا وزن صحیح نہیں آتا اور مجاز کے ساتھ شعر موزون ہو جاتا ہے تو حقیقہ کو چھوڑ کر مجاز کو اختیار کیا جاتا ہے والسمع سمع بندی نشریں ہوتی ہے اور وزن کی پابندی شعر میں فاذا كان السمع جب کسی نثر کی مجمع بندی دال پر جارہی ہے جیسے الاحد والعدد تو یہاں لفظ اسد مجمع میں مستقیم آتا ہے جو کہ مجاز ہے لفظ شجاع نہیں آسکتا جو کہ حقیقت ہے واضاف البدائع اور جو فن بدیع کے اقسام از قیم محسنات لفظیہ ہیں تجنیس یعنی دونوں کلمے لفظی لحاظ سے سمجنس ہوں لیکن معنوی لحاظ سے مختلف . بسا اوقات تجنیس لفظ مجاز سے حاصل ہوتی ہے اور لفظ حقیقت سے حاصل نہیں ہوتی ۔ نحو البدعة شرك الشرك فان الشرك پہلا لفظ شرک بفتح الشين والراای حبالہ الصيد بمعنی جال کے ہے اور دوسرا معنی شرک کرنے کے ہے یعنی باری تعالیٰ کے ساتھ شریک کرنا اور ان دونوں کے درمیان اشتقاقی شیعہ موجود ہے مراد یہ ہے کہ بدعہ مبارکی شرک میں سے ہے لفظ حقیقہ۔ یعنی مباری ساتھ تجنیس حاصل نہیں ہو سکتی تھی لہذا لفظ مجاز اختیار کیا گیا یعنی شرک او معناه یعنی اختصاص معنی داعی الی المجاز ہوگا ۔ اب یہاں
The question of the incentive for metaphor. Know that metaphor needs several things. The borrowed-from (musta'ar minhu), i.e., the thing compared to, which is the specific structure, meaning a specific image and form that a predator animal has. And the borrowed-for (musta'ar lahu), i.e., the thing compared, which is the brave man. And the borrowed (musta'ar), i.e., the word that is taken as a loan from the compared-to and used for the compared, that is the word 'lion'. And the relationship ('ilaqah), meaning the aspect of resemblance, which is bravery. And the truthful context clue (qarinah sadiqah), i.e., a clue that prevents and prohibits the intention of the literal meaning. [؟] that is the word [؟] in this statement: 'I saw a lion shooting arrows' – this is a clear clue that the lion does not mean the specific structure of a predator animal, but rather a brave man. And the matter that motivates the use of metaphor: when you intend to report seeing a brave man, the principle is that you say 'I saw a brave man'; when you say instead 'I saw a lion', it is necessary that there be something that motivates you to abandon the original, i.e., the literal usage, and use the opposite of the original, i.e., metaphor. And that motivator (da'i) will be either verbal or meaningful. The verbal motivator includes several things: [؟] the word of metaphor being sweet and familiar; sometimes the literal word is coarse and repulsive to nature, like the word 'khunfuqiq' which is used for [؟]; so you leave it and use the word 'asr' which is metaphor in that meaning, because it is sweeter and more familiar than the mentioned literal expression. Or suitability for poetry: when using the literal word the meter of the poem does not come right, and with metaphor the poem becomes metrical, so the literal is abandoned and metaphor is adopted. And [؟] [؟] when some prose is bound by [؟] like [؟], here the word 'lion' comes straight in the binding, which is metaphor, while the word 'brave' cannot come, which is literal. And the addition of rhetorical figures: from the art of badi' are verbal beautifications such as pun (tajnis), i.e., two words that are similar in form but different in meaning. Sometimes pun is achieved with a metaphorical word and not with a literal word. For example: 'bid'ah shirku al-shirk' – the first 'shirk' with fatha on shin and ra is a hunting net, and the second means polytheism, i.e., associating partners with Allah; between them there is a derivational [؟] similarity. The meaning is that bid'ah is part of polytheism. The literal word [؟] could not achieve pun, so the metaphorical word was chosen, i.e., 'shirk'. And its meaning: the specificity of meaning is a motivator for metaphor. Now here...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the preconditions and incentives for using metaphor (majaz) in Arabic. The author lists five essential components: (1) musta'ar minhu (the source of borrowing, i.e., the thing compared to), (2) musta'ar lahu (the target, i.e., the thing being compared), (3) musta'ar (the borrowed word), (4) 'ilaqah (the relationship between them, e.g., resemblance), and (5) qarinah sadiqah (a context clue indicating that the literal meaning is not intended). After these, the author discusses the da'i (motivation) for using metaphor instead of the literal expression. The motivations are divided into two categories: lafzi (verbal) and ma'nawi (meaning-related). Verbal incentives include the word being sweeter or more familiar, suitability for poetic meter, or achieving rhetorical devices like pun (tajnis). An example is given: in the phrase 'bid'ah shirku al-shirk', the first 'shirk' (trap) is metaphorical and allows a pun with the second 'shirk' (polytheism), which could not be achieved with the literal word. The text breaks off at 'now here...' but the topic continues in subsequent passages.

Key Terms
مستعار منه (musta'ar minhu): The source of the metaphorical borrowing, i.e., the thing that is compared to (like the lion).
مستعار له (musta'ar lahu): The target of the borrowing, i.e., the thing being compared (like the brave man).
علاقة ('ilaqah): The relationship or similarity between the literal and metaphorical meanings, such as resemblance (tashbih).
قرينة (qarinah): A context clue that indicates the metaphorical meaning is intended rather than the literal.
داعي (da'i): The motivation or reason for using metaphor instead of the literal expression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a verbal incentive (da'i lafzi) for using metaphor?
A) The elegance and sweetness of the metaphorical word.
B) Suitability for poetic meter.
C) Achieving a pun (tajnis).
D) Emphasizing the meaning (ma'nawi).

Key Takeaway
Metaphor in Hanafi usul has five essential components and must be motivated by a valid verbal or meaning-based incentive.

The Five Components of Metaphor (Majaz)
Definition of metaphor components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the five essential components of majaz: musta'ar minhu, musta'ar lahu, musta'ar, 'alaqa, and qarina.

Core Idea
Metaphor needs a source, a target, a borrowed word, a relation, and a contextual indicator.

In Hanafi usul, majaz is not arbitrary; it has a precise structure. Understanding this structure is the first step to recognizing when a word is used metaphorically in legal texts. This lesson unpacks the five components using the classic example of 'lion' for a brave man.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the discussion
The passage states that metaphor (majaz) requires several things. List them as the author enumerates them.
The Arabic word 'majaz' is the key.
2
Extract the five components
Find and write down: musta'ar minhu (source of borrowing), musta'ar lahu (target), musta'ar (borrowed word), 'alaqa (relation), and qarina (contextual indicator).
The example of 'asad' (lion) is used.
3
Understand the function of qarina
The qarina prevents the literal meaning. In the example 'ra'aytu asadan yarmi' (I saw a lion shooting), the word 'yarmi' (shooting) is the qarina that blocks the literal meaning and directs to the metaphorical meaning (brave man).
Qarina can be verbal or contextual.
4
Map to legal interpretation
Apply the same framework to a legal text. For example, the word 'salat' in the Quran may be interpreted literally (prayer) or metaphorically (blessing) depending on the presence of a qarina.
This skill is fundamental in istinbat.

بالتعظیم یعنی ہم کسی مقام پر اظہار عظمت کرنا چاہتے ہیں اور مجازی معنی مختص بالتعظیم ہے تو اس وقت مجاز استعمال کیا جائے گا۔ كا ستعادة اسم الى حنیفۃ جیسے امام ابو حنیفہ کا اسم کسی مرد عالم کے لئے استعارہ کریں تو یہ مجاز مختص بالتعظیم ہے تو یہاں مجاز ا لفظ ابو حنیفہ اس کے لئے استعمال کریں گے او التحقیر یا اس کا معنی مختص التحقیر ہے تو اس اظہار حقارہ کے لئے مجازہ کا استعمال کیا جائیگا ۔ جیسے استعارہ صحیح برائے جاہل اور صبح ذباب صغیر کو کہتے ہیں اوالترغیب والترهيب یعنی معنی مجازی مختص بالترغيب والترہیب ہے اور ہمارا مقصود بھی ترغیب و ترهیب ہے تو یہاں مجاز استعمال ہوگا جیسے ماء الحیوۃ یعنی آب حیات کا استعارہ بعض مشروبات کے لئے تاکہ سامع اس کی طرف رغبت و شوق کرے اور جیسے استعارہ سم بعض مطعومات کے لئے تاکہ سامع اس سے نفرت کرے او زیادۃ البیان یا معنی مجازی کو زیادہ بیان کے ساتھ اختصاص ہے یعنی مقصود معنی مجازی کے ساتھ زیادہ واضح ہو جاتا ہے جیسے رایت اسد ایر کی اس کی دلالہ علی الشجاعہ زیادہ واضح ہے نسبت رأيت شجاعا کے فان ذكر الملزوم یہ مجاز کے امین الدلالہ ہونے کی دلیل ہے یعنی ذکر ملزوم یہ خود وجود لازم پر ایک قسم کا بینہ اور شاہد ہے چونکہ مجاز میں اسم ملزوم کا اطلاق على اللازم ہوتا ہے اور وجود ملزوم شاہد علی وجود اللازم ہوتا ہے تو استعمال مجاز ایسے ہوگا جیسے ایک شئے کا دعوی بالبینہ ہو اور حقیقہ دعوی بلائینہ کے درجہ میں ہوگی او تلطف الکلام یہ مرفوع ہے اور اس کا عطف اختصاص لفظ پر ہے یعنی استعمال مجاز کی طرف داعی کبھی تلطف کلام ہوتا ہے یعنی مجاز و استعارہ میں کلام بڑی لطیف ہو جاتی ہے اور حقیقہ میں ایسے نہیں ہوتا جیسے ایک مہ یعنی کو تلہ فیہ حجر موقد یعنی اس کے کچھ حصہ میں آگ سلگ اور چمک رہی ہو تو اس کیلئے یہ استعارہ کیا جائے " بحر من المسک موجہ الذہب " یعنی مسک و کستوری کا بحر ہو، اور اس کی موج سونا و ذہب ہو تو کستوری سیاہ رنگ کی ہوتی ہے ذہب سرخ اور چمک دار ہوتا ہے تو اسی طرح کو ٹلہ سیاہ اور اس کے بیچ چمک سرخ ایک دوسرے کے ساتھ لطیف تشبیہ ہو جاتی ہے۔ فیفید لذة تخييلية تو اس کے اندر تخیلی لذت ہے جو موجب ہے سرعتہ قسم کی اد مطابقة تمامر المراد یہ مرفوع ہے عطف اس کا تلطف الكلام پر ہے یعنی استعمال مجازہ کی طرف داعی کبھی مطابقہ تمام المراد ہوتی ہے ۔ نیمکن اور یہ بھی ممکن ہے کہ اس کا معنی یہ ہو کہ مجازہ تمام مراد کے ساتھ زیادہ واضح الدلالہ ہونے میں یا وضوح الدلالہ کے ناقص ہونے میں مطابق ہو اصل یہ ہے کہ فان دلالة الالفاظ الخ یعنی الفاظ جو موضوع للمعانی ہوتے ہیں ان کی دلالتہ اپنے معانی موضوع لہا پر علی نھج واحد ہوتی ہے۔ اور وضوح و خفا میں مختلف نہیں ہوتی اس لئے کہا جاتا ہے کہ دلالت مطابقی کے لحاظ سے اختلاف دلالت فی الوضوح والخفا نہیں ہوا کرتا فاذا حاولت پھر جب آپ کی قصد یہ ہے
The issue of the cause leading to metaphor. Know that metaphor needs several things: the thing borrowed from (the object of comparison) which is a specific form (the form of a predatory animal); the thing borrowed for (the subject of comparison) which is the brave man; the borrowed word (the word 'lion'); the relation (likeness in bravery); the indicative context that prevents intending the literal meaning (like saying 'I saw a lion shooting' – this clearly shows that 'lion' here does not mean the predatory animal but a brave man). The cause prompting the use of metaphor: when you intend to report seeing a brave man, the original is to say 'I saw a brave man.' When you say instead 'I saw a lion,' there must be something that prompts you to leave the original (literal) and use the non-original (metaphor). That prompt is either verbal or semantic. The verbal prompt includes: the word of metaphor being sweeter and more familiar; sometimes the literal word is uncouth and repulsive (like 'khunfuqaiq' meaning 'a dry plant'), so it is abandoned for the metaphorical word which is sweeter. Or suitability for poetry: if the literal word does not fit the meter but the metaphorical does, metaphor is chosen. Or rhymed prose: if the literal word does not fit the cadence but the metaphorical does. Or rhetorical figures like tajnis (paronomasia): sometimes tajnis is achieved with a metaphorical word but not with the literal, e.g., 'al-bid'atu shirku sh-shirki' the first 'shirk' (meaning snare) allows tajnis with the second 'shirk' (polytheism), which is metaphorical. The semantic prompt: specification of meaning as glorification: if we want to show respect, and the metaphorical meaning is specifically for glorification, we use metaphor, e.g., using the name of Imam Abu Hanifah for a scholar. Or belittlement: if we want to show insignificance, we use metaphor, e.g., calling an ignorant person 'a fly' or 'a small gnat'. Or encouragement and discouragement: when the metaphorical meaning is specific to encourage or discourage, e.g., calling a beverage 'water of life' to make the listener desire it, or poison for a disliked food. Or increased clarity: the metaphorical meaning is more expressive, e.g., 'I saw a lion' indicates bravery more clearly than 'I saw a brave man', because mentioning the necessary (the lion) is like presenting evidence for the necessary (bravery); metaphor is like a claim with evidence, while literal is a claim without. Or elegance of speech: metaphor makes speech more refined, e.g., describing a piece of charcoal that has a glowing ember as 'a sea of musk whose waves are gold' – the black charcoal with red ember resembles black musk with golden waves, giving imaginative pleasure. Or complete correspondence of meaning: the metaphorical word may correspond more perfectly to the intended meaning; the original principle is that words have uniform indication to their meanings, but the degree of clarity can vary, but in terms of correspondence they are equal; however, metaphor may be chosen for perfect correspondence.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Tawdih wa Al-Talwih explains the factors (dawa'i) that necessitate or justify the use of metaphor (majaz) instead of literal expression (haqiqa) in legal and general Arabic discourse. The author first outlines the components of metaphor: the source (musta'ar minhu), the target (musta'ar lahu), the borrowed term (musta'ar), the relation ('alaqa), and the contextual indicator (qarina) that blocks the literal meaning. Using the example of saying 'I saw a lion shooting arrows,' the qarina (shooting arrows) clarifies that 'lion' denotes a brave man, not the animal. The core discussion then turns to the 'da'i' (cause) prompting metaphor. This cause is divided into two categories: verbal (lafzi) and semantic (ma'nawi). Verbal causes include: the metaphorical word being sweeter/more familiar than a harsh literal word (e.g., avoiding 'khunfuqaiq' in favor of 'asad'); poetic meter requiring a particular word; rhymed prose needing a specific cadence; and rhetorical figures like tajnis (paronomasia) that only succeed with a metaphorical word (example from a saying about innovation being a snare of polytheism, where 'shirk' as snare is metaphorical). Semantic causes include: glorification (using the name of a great scholar to honor another), belittlement (calling an ignorant person a fly), encouragement/discouragement (calling something 'water of life' to attract, or 'poison' to repel), increased clarity (metaphor makes the meaning more vivid, like presenting evidence for a claim), elegance (creating refined imagery like 'a sea of musk whose waves are gold' for glowing charcoal), and perfect correspondence (metaphor may match the intended meaning more precisely than the literal term). The passage concludes by noting that all words indicate their meanings in a uniform manner in terms of correspondence, but the choice of metaphor is based on these factors.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a sense other than its original (literal) meaning due to a relation ('alaqa) and a contextual indicator (qarina)
المستعار منه (al-musta'ar minhu): The source of the metaphor; the object from which the image is borrowed (e.g., the predatory animal in 'lion for a brave man')
المستعار له (al-musta'ar lahu): The target of the metaphor; the subject to which the image is applied (e.g., the brave man)
المستعار (al-musta'ar): The borrowed word used metaphorically (e.g., the word 'asad')
العلاقة (al-'alaqa): The relation between the literal and metaphorical meanings, often resemblance (e.g., bravery)
القرينة (al-qarina): A contextual indicator that prevents intending the literal meaning, thereby directing to the metaphorical interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five components of majaz as outlined in the passage?
A) Musta'ar minhu
B) Musta'ar lahu
C) Qarina
D) Ma'nā (meaning)

Key Takeaway
Metaphor in Hanafi usul is defined by five precise components: source, target, borrowed word, relation, and contextual indicator.

Verbal and Semantic Causes for Metaphor (Dawa'i al-Majaz)
Classification of causes for metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the causes (dawa'i) for using metaphor into verbal and semantic categories and give examples of each.

Core Idea
Metaphor is chosen over literal expression for specific verbal or semantic reasons, not arbitrarily.

Why does a speaker or author choose a metaphor when a literal word exists? The passage answers this by identifying two broad categories of causes: verbal (related to the sound or structure of the word) and semantic (related to meaning and effect). Understanding these causes helps us analyze legal texts where figurative language is used deliberately.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two categories
The passage divides the prompt (da'i) for metaphor into lafzi (verbal) and ma'nawi (semantic). First, identify the verbal causes listed: word being sweeter, poetic meter, rhymed prose, and rhetorical figures like tajnis.
Each cause is illustrated with an example.
2
Analyze verbal cause examples
For each verbal cause, write down the example: (1) avoiding unpleasant literal word like 'khunfuqaiq'; (2) poetic meter requiring a specific word; (3) rhymed prose needing cadence; (4) tajnis with 'shirk'.
Tajnis example: al-bid'atu shirku sh-shirki.
3
Identify semantic causes
Now list the semantic causes: glorification, belittlement, encouragement/discouragement, increased clarity, elegance of speech, and perfect correspondence.
These are about the intended effect on the listener.
4
Apply to legal texts
Consider a legal text that uses metaphor, e.g., 'water of life' for a permissible drink. Identify which cause is at work (encouragement). This affects how the text is interpreted (e.g., does it imply obligation or recommendation?).
In Hanafi usul, the cause can influence the ruling derived.

The issue of the cause leading to metaphor. Know that metaphor needs several things: the thing borrowed from (the object of comparison) which is a specific form (the form of a predatory animal); the thing borrowed for (the subject of comparison) which is the brave man; the borrowed word (the word 'lion'); the relation (likeness in bravery); the indicative context that prevents intending the literal meaning (like saying 'I saw a lion shooting' – this clearly shows that 'lion' here does not mean the predatory animal but a brave man). The cause prompting the use of metaphor: when you intend to report seeing a brave man, the original is to say 'I saw a brave man.' When you say instead 'I saw a lion,' there must be something that prompts you to leave the original (literal) and use the non-original (metaphor). That prompt is either verbal or semantic. The verbal prompt includes: the word of metaphor being sweeter and more familiar; sometimes the literal word is uncouth and repulsive (like 'khunfuqaiq' meaning 'a dry plant'), so it is abandoned for the metaphorical word which is sweeter. Or suitability for poetry: if the literal word does not fit the meter but the metaphorical does, metaphor is chosen. Or rhymed prose: if the literal word does not fit the cadence but the metaphorical does. Or rhetorical figures like tajnis (paronomasia): sometimes tajnis is achieved with a metaphorical word but not with the literal, e.g., 'al-bid'atu shirku sh-shirki' the first 'shirk' (meaning snare) allows tajnis with the second 'shirk' (polytheism), which is metaphorical. The semantic prompt: specification of meaning as glorification: if we want to show respect, and the metaphorical meaning is specifically for glorification, we use metaphor, e.g., using the name of Imam Abu Hanifah for a scholar. Or belittlement: if we want to show insignificance, we use metaphor, e.g., calling an ignorant person 'a fly' or 'a small gnat'. Or encouragement and discouragement: when the metaphorical meaning is specific to encourage or discourage, e.g., calling a beverage 'water of life' to make the listener desire it, or poison for a disliked food. Or increased clarity: the metaphorical meaning is more expressive, e.g., 'I saw a lion' indicates bravery more clearly than 'I saw a brave man', because mentioning the necessary (the lion) is like presenting evidence for the necessary (bravery); metaphor is like a claim with evidence, while literal is a claim without. Or elegance of speech: metaphor makes speech more refined, e.g., describing a piece of charcoal that has a glowing ember as 'a sea of musk whose waves are gold' – the black charcoal with red ember resembles black musk with golden waves, giving imaginative pleasure. Or complete correspondence of meaning: the metaphorical word may correspond more perfectly to the intended meaning; the original principle is that words have uniform indication to their meanings, but the degree of clarity can vary, but in terms of correspondence they are equal; however, metaphor may be chosen for perfect correspondence.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Tawdih wa Al-Talwih explains the factors (dawa'i) that necessitate or justify the use of metaphor (majaz) instead of literal expression (haqiqa) in legal and general Arabic discourse. The author first outlines the components of metaphor: the source (musta'ar minhu), the target (musta'ar lahu), the borrowed term (musta'ar), the relation ('alaqa), and the contextual indicator (qarina) that blocks the literal meaning. Using the example of saying 'I saw a lion shooting arrows,' the qarina (shooting arrows) clarifies that 'lion' denotes a brave man, not the animal. The core discussion then turns to the 'da'i' (cause) prompting metaphor. This cause is divided into two categories: verbal (lafzi) and semantic (ma'nawi). Verbal causes include: the metaphorical word being sweeter/more familiar than a harsh literal word (e.g., avoiding 'khunfuqaiq' in favor of 'asad'); poetic meter requiring a particular word; rhymed prose needing a specific cadence; and rhetorical figures like tajnis (paronomasia) that only succeed with a metaphorical word (example from a saying about innovation being a snare of polytheism, where 'shirk' as snare is metaphorical). Semantic causes include: glorification (using the name of a great scholar to honor another), belittlement (calling an ignorant person a fly), encouragement/discouragement (calling something 'water of life' to attract, or 'poison' to repel), increased clarity (metaphor makes the meaning more vivid, like presenting evidence for a claim), elegance (creating refined imagery like 'a sea of musk whose waves are gold' for glowing charcoal), and perfect correspondence (metaphor may match the intended meaning more precisely than the literal term). The passage concludes by noting that all words indicate their meanings in a uniform manner in terms of correspondence, but the choice of metaphor is based on these factors.

Key Terms
داعي لفظي (da'i lafzi): A verbal cause prompting metaphor, such as word sweetness, poetic meter, rhymed prose, or rhetorical figures.
داعي معنوي (da'i ma'nawi): A semantic cause prompting metaphor, such as glorification, belittlement, encouragement, discouragement, increased clarity, elegance, or perfect correspondence.
تجني (tajnis): Paronomasia or wordplay using similar-sounding words with different meanings, often achieved through metaphor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Metaphor in Hanafi usul is a motivated choice based on verbal or semantic needs, not arbitrary decoration.

Causes for Using Majaz over Haqiqah in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the six motivations for choosing majaz over haqiqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six classical incentives (dawa'i) for using majaz and apply them to analyze why a mujtahid might choose majaz over haqiqah in textual interpretation.

Core Idea
Majaz is not arbitrary—it serves specific communicative purposes: glorification, belittlement, persuasion, clarity, elegance, and precision of intent.

This passage explains why a speaker or legal interpreter might deliberately choose figurative language over literal. Understanding these motivations is essential for analyzing the ijtihad of the imams, as they often employed majaz to convey meaning with greater impact or precision. The Hanafi school considers majaz a legitimate and even necessary tool when contextual clues (qarina) are present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six dawa'i
Read the passage and list the six incentives: ta'zim, tahqir, targhib/tarhib, ziyadat al-bayan, talattuf al-kalam, mutabaqat tamam al-murad.
Each has a specific Arabic term and function.
2
Understand each da'i with example
For each incentive, note the example given. For instance, ta'zim is illustrated by using 'Abu Hanifa' for a learned man, and targhib by using 'water of life' for a beverage.
The examples clarify how each da'i operates.
3
Analyze the clarity argument
The author responds to the objection that majaz causes confusion. Identify the role of qarina and the claim that majaz can be clearer than haqiqah, especially when the borrowed term is sensory and the intended meaning is rational.
This strengthens the legitimacy of majaz in legal interpretation.
4
Relate to usul al-fiqh
Consider why a mujtahid needs to know these dawa'i. They help interpret ambiguous texts, justify metaphorical readings, and understand the imams' choices in istinbat.
This shows the practical importance beyond rhetoric.
5
Apply to a new example
Choose a da'i not explicitly used in the text (e.g., talattuf) and create a hypothetical scenario where a mujtahid would prefer majaz for that purpose.
Tests comprehension and ability to transfer knowledge.

ساتھ مافی القلوب کی کنہ اور پوری حقیقت واضح کر دے خانك اذا اردت جب آپ ارادہ کرتے ہیں۔ کہ ایک شئے کی وصف بالسواد کو ایک مقدار مخصوص پر بیان کرنا چاہتے ہیں ۔ فاصل المراد تو اصل مراد تو یہ ہے کہ آپ اس کی کمیتہ سواد اور اس کے مقدار پر یہ لفظ دال نہیں ہو سکتا فلا بد تو پس ضروری ہے کوئی ایسی شئی ذکر کریں جس کے ساتھ سامع کو کمیتہ سواد اور مقدار سواد کی پوری معرفت ہو جائے تو آپ اسی شتی کے ساتھ اس کو تشبیہ دیں گے ۔ اور اس کے لئے اس شئے مخصوص کا استعارہ کریں گے تاکہ سامع کے سامنے آپ کی تمام مراد واضح ہو جائے ۔ وغير ذلك یہ بھی مرفوع سے یعنی داعی الی المجاز کبھی غیر ماذکرنا ہوتا ہے ۔ مما ذكرنا في مقدمة كتاب الوشاح به كتاب الوشاح مصنف کی کتاب ہے اس کے مقدم اور فصل تشبیہ اور فصل مجاز میں مذکور ہیں مقدمہ اور فصل تشبیہ میں ذکر کیا ہے کہ غرض من التشبیہ ماھو یعنی کیا ہوتی ہے اور کبھی وہ عرض خود برائے استعارہ ہوتی ہے اور فصل مجانہ میں ذکر کیا کہ مجاز کبھی مفید نہیں ہوتا اور کبھی مفید ہوتا ہے اور مبالغہ فی التشبیہ نہیں ہوتا اور کبھی مفید ہوتا ہے اور اس کے اندر مبالغہ في التشبیہ بھی ہوتا ہے جیسے استعارہ میں ہوتا ہے۔ فصل وتد تجرى الاستعارة التبعية في الحروف علما بيان نے ذکر کیا ہے کہ استعارہ دو قسم ہے
Explanation of the meaning-based incentive begins. By ta'zim: when we intend to express glorification and the metaphorical meaning is specific to glorification, then majaz is used. For example, using the name 'Abu Hanifa' metaphorically for a learned man, this is majaz specific to glorification. By tahqir: when the meaning is specific to belittlement, majaz is used for expressing contempt. By targhib and tarhib: when the metaphorical meaning is specific to encouraging or deterring and our intention is also that, then majaz is used, like 'water of life' for some beverages to attract the listener, or 'poison' for some foods to repel. By ziyadat al-bayan: the metaphorical meaning is specific to increased clarity, e.g., 'I saw a lion' conveys courage more clearly than 'I saw a brave man'. Since mentioning the necessary (malzum) serves as evidence for the existence of the necessary (lazim), the use of majaz is like a claim with proof, while haqiqah is like a claim without proof. By talattuf al-kalam: sometimes the incentive is elegance, as when describing a black rock with fire inside as 'a sea of musk whose waves are gold', creating an imaginative pleasure. By mutabaqat tamam al-murad: sometimes majaz is used to convey the complete intended meaning, which the literal word cannot. The indication of words to their meanings is uniform, but if one wants to convey a meaning with clearer or more obscure indication, one must use majaz, because majaz comes in many forms, some clearer, some more obscure. If it is objected that majaz causes confusion, the reply is that the confusion is removed by the context (qarina) which is a condition for majaz. When the thing from which metaphor is taken (musta'ar minhu) is a sensory object and the thing for which metaphor is used (musta'ar lahu) is rational, then majaz is clearer than haqiqah because haqiqah indicates a rational concept while majaz presents it as sensory. Also, mentioning the malzum is itself evidence for the existence of the lazim, and majaz causes quick understanding. The meaning of mutabaqat tamam al-murad can also be that the speaker clarifies the full reality of what is in his heart. For example, if you want to describe something's blackness with a specific measure, the literal word only indicates the basic blackness, not the measure, so you must use a metaphor comparing it to something that conveys the measure. Other incentives are mentioned as from the book Al-Wishah, discussing the purpose of tashbih and the benefits of majaz, including whether it includes exaggeration or not.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary explains the various motivations (dawa'i) that justify using metaphorical language (majaz) instead of literal (haqiqah). The author lists six distinct incentives: ta'zim (glorification), tahqir (belittlement), targhib/tarhib (encouragement/deterrence), ziyadat al-bayan (increased clarity), talattuf al-kalam (elegance), and mutabaqat tamam al-murad (fulfilling the full intention). Each is illustrated with examples. Notably, the example using the name 'Abu Hanifa' for a learned man shows respect. The passage then addresses the claim that majaz might be confusing, clarifying that the presence of a contextual clue (qarina) removes any ambiguity. It further argues that majaz can be clearer than haqiqah when the borrowed term is sensory and the intended meaning is rational, as the metaphor makes the rational concept perceptible. The reference to Al-Wishah indicates this discussion is part of a broader rhetorical tradition. For the Hanafi student, this reinforces that majaz is a legitimate and often necessary tool in legal interpretation and language, not a mere deviation.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Using a word in a meaning other than its original literal meaning due to a contextual connection (qarina).
حقیقت (haqiqah): Using a word in its originally assigned literal meaning.
قرینہ (qarina): A contextual clue that indicates the intended meaning in majaz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as a reason (da'i) for using majaz?
A) Ta'zim (glorification)
B) Tahqir (belittlement)
C) Takhfif (lightening)
D) Targhib (encouragement)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes six specific motivations for using majaz, and the presence of a contextual clue (qarina) ensures that majaz is not confusing but can be clearer than literal language.

When Metaphor Becomes Clearer Than Literal
Explaining the clarity of majaz over haqiqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions under which majaz is clearer than haqiqah and apply the usul principle to identify when a metaphor clarifies an abstract meaning.

Core Idea
Metaphor can be clearer than literal when it presents abstract meanings through well-known sensory examples.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, we often assume the literal word is the clearest. However, this passage challenges that assumption, showing that metaphor (majaz) can sometimes be even clearer than literal (haqiqah). The key lies in the context (qarina) and the relationship between the sensory borrowed-from and the intellectual borrowed-for.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Recognize that the passage asserts a counterintuitive rule: majaz can be clearer than haqiqah. This is the main usul rule being expounded.
The claim is not absolute; it depends on specific conditions.
2
Understand the objection and response
The passage anticipates an objection that majaz is confusing. The response is that the confusion is removed by the context (qarina), which is a necessary condition for majaz.
Qarina is essential; without it, the word remains literal.
3
Analyze the rational proof
The author provides a rational argument: when the borrowed-from (musta'ar minhu) is a well-known sensory object and the borrowed-for (musta'ar lahu) is an abstract meaning, the metaphor presents the abstract as if sensory, making it clearer.
This is an aqli dalil, not based on a transmitted text.
4
Apply the rule to an example
Consider the example of describing the quantity of blackness. The literal word for blackness only indicates blackness, but a metaphor comparing it to a specific black object clarifies the exact shade and quantity.
The metaphor achieves 'tamam al-murad' (complete intent) beyond 'asl al-murad' (original intent).
5
Note additional clarifications
The passage also mentions that majaz can cause quick understanding and that the purpose of simile and metaphor is elaborated in the book Al-Washshah.
This shows the topic is part of a broader discussion in Hanafi usul literature.

بالسواد المخصوص کو پیش کریں فا للفظ الموضوع پس وہ لفظ جو معنی سواد کے لئے موضوع ہے وہ تو صرف اصل مراد پر دلالت کرے گا ۔ لیکن وہ تمام مراد یعنی بیان کمیتہ سواد اور اس کے مقدار پر یہ لفظ دال نہیں ہو سکتا فلا بد تو پس ضروری ہے کوئی ایسی شئی ذکر کریں جس کے ساتھ سامع کو کمیتہ سواد اور مقدار سواد کی پوری معرفت ہو جائے تو آپ اسی شتی کے ساتھ اس کو تشبیہ دیں گے ۔ اور اس کے لئے اس شئے مخصوص کا استعارہ کریں گے تاکہ سامع کے سامنے آپ کی تمام مراد واضح ہو جائے ۔ وغير ذلك یہ بھی مرفوع سے یعنی داعی الی المجاز کبھی غیر ماذکرنا ہوتا ہے ۔ مما ذكرنا في مقدمة كتاب الوشاح به كتاب الوشاح مصنف کی کتاب ہے اس کے مقدم اور فصل تشبیہ اور فصل مجاز میں مذکور ہیں مقدمہ اور فصل تشبیہ میں ذکر کیا ہے کہ غرض من التشبیہ ماھو یعنی کیا ہوتی ہے اور کبھی وہ عرض خود برائے استعارہ ہوتی ہے اور فصل مجانہ میں ذکر کیا کہ مجاز کبھی مفید نہیں ہوتا اور کبھی مفید ہوتا ہے اور مبالغہ فی التشبیہ نہیں ہوتا اور کبھی مفید ہوتا ہے اور اس کے اندر مبالغہ في التشبیہ بھی ہوتا ہے جیسے استعارہ میں ہوتا ہے۔
That you want to express a meaning with a signification that is clearer or more hidden relative to the literal word, so it is necessary to use the metaphorical word. Then it is said: Metaphors are numerous; some of them are clearer in signification and some are hidden. If it is asked: How can the signification of metaphor be clearer than that of the literal word? Rather, metaphor is confusing to understanding. We reply: The confusion to understanding is removed by the context. Context is a condition in metaphor. Then, when the thing borrowed from is a sensory matter, and among sensory things that are characterized by the intended meaning, the thing borrowed from is the most well-known of sensory things, and the thing borrowed for is an intellectual matter, then certainly metaphor is clearer than literal. Because the literal indicates an intellectual matter, but metaphor shows it as if it were sensory, so it is certainly clearer. And also what we mentioned, etc., meaning additionally that mentioning the necessary is itself like the proof and witness for the existence of the necessary, and this is also evidence for clarity of signification. And additionally, metaphor causes quick understanding. These are additional reasons that metaphor can be clearer in signification than literal. And it is possible that the meaning of complete correspondence is that the speaker clarifies the essence of what is in the heart and the whole reality through his verbal expression. For example, when you intend to describe a thing's blackness with a specific quantity. The original intent is that you present its blackness, but the complete intent is that you present its description with the specific blackness. So the word that is coined for the meaning of blackness will only indicate the original intent. But it cannot indicate the complete intent, i.e., expressing its quantity of blackness and its measure. So it is necessary to mention something through which the listener obtains full knowledge of the quantity and measure of blackness. Then you will liken it to that thing. And for that, you will use a metaphor of that specific thing so that your complete intent becomes clear to the listener. And other than that, the cause for metaphor is sometimes other than what we mentioned, as we mentioned in the introduction of the book Al-Washshah. The book Al-Washshah is a book by the author. In its introduction, chapter on simile, and chapter on metaphor, it is mentioned. In the introduction and chapter on simile, it is mentioned that the purpose of simile is such-and-such, and sometimes it is for metaphor itself. And in the chapter on metaphor, it is mentioned that metaphor is sometimes not beneficial, and sometimes beneficial without exaggeration in simile, and sometimes beneficial with exaggeration in simile, as occurs in metaphor.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage discusses a rule in usul al-fiqh: metaphor (majaz) can be clearer in signification than the literal word (haqiqah). The author addresses a potential objection that metaphor is confusing. He responds that the confusion is removed by the context (qarina), which is a necessary condition for metaphor. He then gives a rational argument: if the borrowed-from (musta'ar minhu) is a well-known sensory object and the borrowed-for (musta'ar lahu) is an abstract meaning, the metaphor is clearer because it presents the abstract as sensory. For example, describing the quantity of blackness by comparing it to a well-known black object. The author also mentions that the purpose of metaphor can be to express the full intent (tamam al-murad) beyond the original intent (asl al-murad). He refers to the book Al-Washshah for further details.

Key Terms
حقیقہ (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word as originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in other than its original meaning with a contextual indicator (qarina).
قرینہ (qarina): Contextual evidence that indicates the intended metaphorical meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is metaphor (majaz) clearer than the literal word (haqiqah)?
A) The musta'ar minhu must be abstract and the musta'ar lahu sensory.
B) The musta'ar minhu must be a well-known sensory object and the musta'ar lahu an intellectual meaning.
C) The musta'ar minhu must be from the Quran.
D) The musta'ar lahu must be a well-known sensory object.

Key Takeaway
Majaz can be clearer than haqiqah when it presents abstract meanings through well-known sensory objects, provided the context (qarina) is present.
